(Note: Another source indicates the initial altitude was 574 km, though the orbital parameters are similar.)\n\n## Mission Purpose and Instruments\nThe Solar Maximum Mission was designed to provide coordinated observations of solar activity, in particular solar flares, during a period of maximum solar activity. The payload consisted of seven instruments, specifically selected to study the short-wavelength and coronal manifestations of flares.\n\n## Notable Facts\nThe SMM observatory was of modular construction and measured approximately 4 m in length, fitting into a circular envelope 2.3 m in diameter. SMM was notable in that it was the first orbiting, uncrewed satellite to be repaired in space, and during STS-41-C in April 1984, astronauts James van Hoften and George Nelson successfully repaired it, adding five more years to the lifespan of the satellite.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"cfa2c7bb-5bc9-4b56-a30e-fb2395d8abca","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:05.814Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"31c9374e-be19-4c53-b753-75eb2c554ee4","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Engle and Richard H. Truly, launched on November 12, 1981, and landed two days later on November 14, 1981. STS-2 marked the first time that a crewed, reusable orbital vehicle returned to space.\n\n## Crew\nThe flight crew consisted of Colonel Joe H. Engle, United States Air Force, and Captain Richard H. Truly, United States Navy. The launch day coincided with Truly's 44th birthday, and he became the first person ever to fly to space on his birthday.\n\n## Launch and Landing Details\nSpace Shuttle Columbia launched on Nov. 12, 1981 at 10:09:59 a.m. EST from Launch Pad 39A. Landing occurred on Nov. 14, 1981 at 1:23:11 p.m. PST on Runway 23, with a rollout distance of 7,711 feet and rollout time of 53 seconds, during the 37th Revolution with a mission duration of 2 days, 6 hours, 13 minutes and 12 seconds. They brought Columbia safely home to a landing at NASA's Dryden Flight Research Facility, now NASA's Armstrong Flight Research Facility, at Edwards Air Force Base in California.\n\n## Mission Duration and Challenges\nTheir planned five-day mission had to be curtailed to just two days following the failure of one of Columbia's three fuel cells. The original 125 hours was reduced to 54 hours.\n\n## Payloads\nColumbia carried the OSTA-1 payload (named for the NASA Office of Space and Terrestrial Applications), which consisted of a number of remote-sensing instruments mounted on a Spacelab pallet in the payload bay. These instruments, including the Shuttle Imaging Radar-A (SIR-A), successfully carried out remote sensing of Earth's resources, environmental quality, and ocean and weather conditions. In addition, the Canadian-built \"Canadarm\" Remote Manipulator System (RMS) was successfully operated in all its various operating modes for the first time.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"c5872732-74eb-410a-bf48-f115c8b09550","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:29.274Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"225ac4f2-cf98-4d5c-b0ea-95210b158f90","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mission, crewed by Jack R. Lousma and C. Gordon Fullerton, involved extensive orbital endurance testing of Columbia itself, as well as numerous scientific experiments.\n\n## Launch Details\nAt precisely 11 a.m. EST on March 22, 1982, space shuttle Columbia lifted off from Launch Pad 39A, riding on the thrust of its two solid rocket boosters and its three main engines. Launch was delayed one hour due to failure of heater on nitrogen gas ground support line.\n\n## Payloads\nOne of the first tasks for Lousma and Fullerton involved activating the Office of Space Science-1 (OSS-1) payload, a suite of nine experiments in space plasma physics, solar physics, astronomy, life sciences, and space technology located in Columbia's payload bay and one in the middeck. Payloads included Office of Space Science (OSS) experiments, Monodisperse Latex Reactor (MLR), Electrophoresis Verification Test (EEVT), Plant Lignification Experiment.\n\n## Landing at White Sands\nSTS-3 was the first shuttle launch with an unpainted external tank and the only mission to land at the White Sands Space Harbor near Alamogordo, New Mexico. The orbiter was forced to land at White Sands due to flooding at its originally planned landing site, Edwards Air Force Base. On March 30, 1982, the space shuttle Columbia, with astronauts Jack R. Lousma and C. Gordon Fullerton aboard, landed at the Northrup strip on White Sands Missile Range in New Mexico. Columbia made 130 orbits and traveled 5,300,000 km (3,300,000 mi) during its 8-day 0-hour 4-minute 46-second flight.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b21e9364-c851-4a76-8a79-3bc365046434","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:43.168Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6198f4f9-01ac-458a-9560-26b370c97791","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here are the key details:\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nSTS-4 launched from Kennedy Space Center on June 27, 1982, at 15:00:00 UTC, with Ken Mattingly as commander and Henry Hartsfield as pilot.\n\n## Key Mission Characteristics\n\nSTS-4 was the final test flight for the Space Shuttle; it was thereafter officially declared to be operational. STS-4, being the last test flight of the Space Shuttle, was also the last to carry a crew of two astronauts.\n\nThis mission marked the first time the Space Shuttle launched precisely at its scheduled launch time.\n\n## DOD Payload\n\nThe STS-4 mission, the last of the program's four orbital flight tests, featured the first shuttle payload sponsored by the Department of Defense (DOD). Mattingly, an active-duty naval officer, later described the classified payload – two sensors for detecting missile launches – as a \"rinky-dink collection of minor stuff they wanted to fly\".\n\n## Landing and Mission Duration\n\nColumbia landed on July 4, 1982, at 16:09:31 UTC, on the 15,000 ft concrete runway 22 at Edwards Air Force Base, the first orbital Shuttle landing on a concrete runway. The flight lasted 7 days, 1 hour, 9 minutes, and 31 seconds, and covered a total distance of 4,700,000 km in 112 complete orbits.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8e51e212-6abc-4c48-b406-2bf16cddc539","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:56.173Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"497268bc-d5ac-4a26-80e7-f7fad79c0b59","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## STS-5 Mission Overview\n\nOn its fifth trip into space in November 1982, space shuttle Columbia's STS-5 mission set a number of firsts, with NASA managers considering this the first operational flight of the space shuttle following completion of the four Orbital Flight Tests.\n\n## Crew Members\n\nThe crew consisted of astronauts Vance D. Brand (commander), Robert F. Overmyer (pilot), and Joseph P. Allen and William B. Lenoir (both mission specialists). This was the first time four astronauts rode into space in the same spacecraft, and Joseph P. Allen and William B. Lenoir were the first two mission specialists to fly.\n\n## Launch and Landing\n\nAt 7:19 a.m. EST on Nov. 11, 1982, space shuttle Columbia lifted off from Launch Pad 39A, carrying its crew of four astronauts and payload of two commercial satellites. Columbia landed on Runway 22 at Edwards Air Force Base on November 16, 1982, at 06:33:26 a.m. PST, having traveled 3,397,082 km (2,110,849 mi) in 81 orbits during a mission that lasted 5 days, 2 hours, 14 minutes and 26 seconds.\n\n## Satellite Deployment\n\nThis first shuttle operational mission deployed two commercial communications satellites, ANIK C-3 for TELESAT Canada and SBS-C for Satellite Business Systems. At about eight hours into the mission, Lenoir deployed the SBS-3 satellite from the shuttle's payload bay. The satellite's attached two-stage Payload Assist Module-D (PAM-D) kick motor then fired to begin the process of placing it into its 22,300-mile-high geosynchronous operational orbit.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"cffcb16d-e8bf-4269-9452-21167693c78f","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:10.145Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"3198af7a-af26-46a7-85bd-650886fe0018","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Solar flux measurement\n\n## Key Results\nThe ACRIM instrument revealed that \"the Sun is actually brighter during the sunspot cycle maximum\" due to bright faculae surrounding sunspots offsetting darkening effects.\n\n## STS-41-C Repair Mission (April 1984)\n- Astronauts James van Hoften and George Nelson conducted in-orbit repairs\n- Replaced the entire attitude control system module and coronagraph electronics\n- Installed gas cover over X-ray polychromator\n- Extended satellite lifespan by approximately five years\n- Notable achievement: \"first orbiting, uncrewed satellite to be repaired in space\"\n\n## Comet Discoveries\n10 Kreutz sungrazer comets discovered, with nuclei estimated at no greater than 16 m radius.\n\n## End of Mission\n**Reentry:** December 2, 1989, over the Indian Ocean\n**Attitude Control Loss:** November 17, 1989\n\n**Mission Duration:** 9 years, 9 months, 18 days"}]},"uuid":"4ef63f19-3845-4d5c-abed-655bf5b25fad","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:32.431Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c0bce7a6-c0a3-4c65-93bb-08cb1a198235","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

{"parentUuid":"e93855bb-e800-45fd-9b7a-47c2e23e4ed4","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"ea4b4095-6634-4f61-a4ea-252488a65bc6","agentId":"a839aee13819dce56","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_017qdTixDmXtqhPU5dANh1Ba","type":"tool_result","content":"Web search results for query: \"IRAS Infrared Astronomical Satellite 1983 launch details mass orbit instruments discoveries\"\n\nLinks: [{\"title\":\"IRAS - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IRAS\"},{\"title\":\"Infrared Astronomical Satellite | Astronomy, Space Exploration, Infrared Imaging | Britannica\",\"url\":\"https://www.britannica.com/topic/Infrared-Astronomical-Satellite\"},{\"title\":\"The Infrared Astronomical Satellite (IRAS) Mission. - ADS\",\"url\":\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1984ApJ...278L...1N/abstract\"},{\"title\":\"Infrared Astronomical Satellite (IRAS) Catalogs and Atlases.Volume 1: Explanatory Supplement. - ADS\",\"url\":\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1988iras....1.....B/abstract\"},{\"title\":\"Infrared Astronomical Satellite Iras\",\"url\":\"https://www.jpl.nasa.gov/missions/infrared-astronomical-satellite-iras/\"},{\"title\":\"IRAS\",\"url\":\"https://space.skyrocket.de/doc_sdat/iras.htm\"},{\"title\":\"IRAS\",\"url\":\"http://astro.vaporia.com/start/iras.html\"},{\"title\":\"Introduction to the Infrared Astronomical Satellite (IRAS)\",\"url\":\"https://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/IRASdocs/iras.html\"},{\"title\":\"IRAS, the Infrared Astronomical Satellite | Nature\",\"url\":\"https://www.nature.com/articles/303287a0\"},{\"title\":\"Infrared Space Observatory - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared_Space_Observatory\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the IRAS mission:\n\n## Launch Details\nThe IRAS was launched on 25 January 1983, with a mission lasting ten months. It was launched on a Delta rocket from Vandenberg Air Force Base in California into a polar orbit at an altitude of 900 km (550 miles).\n\n## Partnership and Satellite Properties\nThe telescope was a joint project of the United States (NASA), the Netherlands (NIVR), and the United Kingdom (SERC). Its 60-cm-diameter telescope was cooled by superfluid helium that chilled the structure down to 10 K (−263 °C) and the detector to 2 K (−271 °C). 73 kilograms (161 lb) of superfluid helium kept the telescope at a temperature of 2 K, keeping the satellite cool by evaporation.\n\n## Instruments\nIRAS contained a 0.6 m Ritchey-Chretien telescope with an array of 62 detectors used to detect infrared flux in bands centered at 12, 25, 60, and 100 micrometers. In addition to the focal-plane detector array used for the all-sky survey, a low-resolution spectrometer and a 60 and 100 micrometer chopped photometric channel were included on IRAS.\n\n## Key Discoveries\nIt discovered about 350,000 sources, many of which are still awaiting identification. This led to the discovery of three asteroids, including 3200 Phaethon (an Apollo asteroid and the parent body of the Geminid meteor shower), six comets, and a huge dust trail associated with comet 10P/Tempel. New discoveries included a dust disk around Vega and the first images of the Milky Way's core.\n\n## Mission End\nThe on-board supply of liquid helium was depleted after 10 months on 21 November 1983, causing the telescope temperature to rise, preventing further observations.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b0ad64d4-c2b1-4170-bb52-5aac54d0f6e4","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:50.474Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e93855bb-e800-45fd-9b7a-47c2e23e4ed4","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/history/40-years-ago-first-flight-of-space-shuttle-challenger/\"},{\"title\":\"STS-6 | National Aeronautics and Space Administration Wiki | Fandom\",\"url\":\"https://nasa.fandom.com/wiki/STS-6\"}]\n\nBased on the web search results, here are the key details about STS-6, Space Shuttle Challenger's first flight:\n\n## Mission Overview\nSTS-6, the first mission of the orbiter Challenger, lifted off from Launch Complex 39A at the Kennedy Space Center on April 4, 1983, at 18:30:00 UTC (1:30 pm EST).\n\n## Crew\nThe crew consisted of Paul J. Weitz (commander), Karol J. Bobko (pilot), Story Musgrave and Donald H. Peterson (both mission specialists). Commander Paul Weitz had previously served as Pilot on the first Skylab crewed mission (Skylab-2), where he lived and worked in Skylab for nearly a month from May to June 1973.\n\n## TDRS Deployment\nLaunch of STS-6 occurred April 4, 1983, and about 10 hours into the mission, the crew deployed the TDRS/IUS stack from the cargo bay, successfully completing the primary objective of the flight. However, although the 2,300 kg (5,100 lb) TDRS-A satellite was successfully deployed from Challenger, its two-stage booster rocket, the Inertial Upper Stage (IUS), tumbled out of control, placing the satellite into a low elliptical orbit. The satellite contained extra propellant beyond what was needed for its attitude control thrusters, and during the next several months, its thrusters were fired at carefully planned intervals, gradually moving TDRS-1 into its geosynchronous operating orbit, thus saving the US$100-million satellite.\n\n## EVA Details\nUsing new spacesuits designed specifically for the Space Shuttle program, Musgrave and Peterson successfully accomplished the program's first extravehicular activity (EVA) on April 7–8, 1983, performing various tests in the orbiter's payload bay. Their spacewalk lasted 4 hours and 17 minutes.\n\n## Mission Duration and Landing\nChallenger returned to Earth on April 9, 1983, coming to a stop on Runway 22 at Edwards Air Force Base at 18:53:42 UTC (10:53:42 a.m. PST, local time at the landing site).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"50881382-8b54-4c5b-8b61-a4d1332b7c30","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:03.463Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a58424d7-936c-49a4-8c5e-c9a097fb483a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/history/sally-ride-first-american-woman-in-space/\"},{\"title\":\"Ride, Sally Ride: Remembering the Legacy of America’s First Woman in Space, OTD in 1983 - AmericaSpace\",\"url\":\"https://www.americaspace.com/2024/06/18/ride-sally-ride-remembering-the-legacy-of-americas-first-woman-in-space-otd-in-1983/\"},{\"title\":\"“You’re Not In A Simulator”: Remembering the Ride of Sally Ride & the Achievements of Women in Space - AmericaSpace\",\"url\":\"https://www.americaspace.com/2023/06/18/youre-not-in-a-simulator-remembering-the-ride-of-sally-ride-the-achievements-of-women-in-space/\"},{\"title\":\"June 18, 1983 Launch of STS-7 - NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/centers/marshall/history/ride_140618.html\"},{\"title\":\"Sally Ride – Irene North\",\"url\":\"https://irenenorth.com/writings/2020/03/08/sally-ride/\"},{\"title\":\"Sally Ride, first American woman in space | BBC Sky at Night Magazine\",\"url\":\"https://www.skyatnightmagazine.com/space-missions/sally-ride\"}]\n\nHere's the comprehensive information about the STS-7 mission:\n\n## Mission Overview\nOn June 18, 1983, space shuttle Challenger lifted off on its second journey to space, the STS-7 mission. Among its five-person crew, Challenger carried the first American woman into space, NASA astronaut Sally K. Ride.\n\n## Crew\nNASA named Robert L. Crippen as commander of the flight, the first astronaut assigned to a second shuttle mission, Frederick \"Rick\" H. Hauck as the pilot, and John M. Fabian and Sally K. Ride as the two mission specialists. NASA added physician-astronaut Norman E. Thagard to the crew in January 1983 to conduct in-flight studies of space motion sickness. Other notable firsts for the mission included the first five-person crew, the first flight of astronauts from the Class of 1978, and the first astronaut to make a second flight on the shuttle.\n\n## Payloads\nTwo communications satellites – Anik C2 for Telesat of Canada, and Palapa B1 for Indonesia – were successfully deployed during the first two days of the mission. \n\nThe mission also carried the first Shuttle pallet satellite (SPAS-1), which was built by the West German aerospace firm Messerschmitt-Bölkow-Blohm (MBB). SPAS-1 was unique in that it was designed to operate in the payload bay or be deployed by the Remote Manipulator System (Canadarm) as a free-flying satellite. Ride operated the robotic arm to deploy and retrieve SPAS-1.\n\nSTS-7 also carried seven Getaway Special (GAS) canisters, which contained a wide variety of experiments, as well as the OSTA-2 payload, a joint U.S.-West Germany scientific pallet payload.\n\n## Mission Duration and Landing\nThe mission lasted 6 days, 2 hours, 23 minutes, and 59 seconds, and covered about 3,570,000 km (2,220,000 mi) during 97 orbits of the Earth. Unacceptable weather forced a change to Runway 15 at Edwards Air Force Base. The landing took place on June 24, 1983, at 06:56:59 a.m.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"cdc6e921-b15c-4609-bfae-c1ce519dfbe0","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:18.131Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f8848fcb-46e3-44ef-a77a-b90c590c2f61","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

He served as mission specialist on the six-day flight, which featured the first night launch and night landing of the shuttle program.\n\n## Crew Composition\n\nThe STS-8 crew consisted of Commander Richard H. Truly, Pilot Daniel C. Brandenstein, and Mission Specialists Dale A. Gardner, Guion \"Guy\" S. Bluford, and Dr. William E. Thornton.\n\n## Primary and Secondary Payloads\n\nThe mission's primary payload was INSAT-1B, an Indian communications and weather observation satellite, which was released by the orbiter and boosted into a geostationary orbit. The secondary payload, replacing a delayed NASA communications satellite, was a four-metric-ton dummy payload, intended to test the use of the shuttle's Canadarm (remote manipulator system).\n\n## Mission Duration\n\nIt launched on August 30, 1983, and landed on September 5, 1983, conducting the first night launch and night landing of the Space Shuttle program.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"deb8930a-a03a-41bd-8690-7c2c030056f8","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:29.250Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"ab9b3112-9627-442f-85ac-9f0d89b24147","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mission deployed two communications satellites.\n\n## First Untethered Spacewalk\n\nOn February 7, the fourth day of the mission, astronauts McCandless and Stewart performed the first untethered spacewalk, operating the Manned Maneuvering Unit (MMU) for the first time. At 8:25 a.m. EST, McCandless ventured out of Challenger's payload bay and reached 98 m (322 ft) from the orbiter.\n\nThe MMU was a self-propelled and untethered backpack in space, contrasting with previous spacewalkers who remained connected to the vehicle with tethers. McCandless's first EVA lasted 6 hours and 17 minutes, while Stewart's second EVA lasted 5 hours and 55 minutes.\n\n## Crew Details\n\nThe STS-41-B crew included commander Vance D. Brand, pilot Robert L. Gibson, and mission specialists Bruce McCandless II, Ronald E. McNair, and Robert L. This was the first mission in which a satellite, the malfunctioning Solar Maximum Mission, was repaired in Earth orbit.\n\n## Crew Details\n\nThe five-person crew consisted of Commander Robert L. \"Crip\" Crippen, Pilot Francis R. \"Dick\" Scobee, and Mission Specialists Terry J. \"TJ\" Hart, James D. \"Ox\" Van Hoften, and George D. \"Pinky Nelson. Crippen was the flight's only veteran, and for the other four astronauts, all selected as astronauts in 1978, STS-41C represented their first trip into space.\n\n## Mission Objectives\n\nThe mission had two primary objectives: the deployment of the LDEF, and the retrieval, repair, and release of the Solar Maximum Mission satellite, Solar Max for short.\n\n## EVA and Satellite Repair\n\nNelson, using the Manned Maneuvering Unit (MMU), flew out to the satellite and attempted to grasp it with a special capture tool called the Trunnion Pin Acquisition Device (TPAD). Three attempts to clamp the TPAD onto the satellite failed, and Solar Max began tumbling on multiple axes when Nelson attempted to grab one of the satellite's solar arrays by hand.\n\nNelson and van Hoften then began the repair operation, replacing the satellite's attitude control mechanism and the main electronics system of the coronagraph instrument. The ultimately successful repair effort took two separate spacewalks.\n\n## Mission Duration and Landing\n\nThe mission lasted 6 days, 23 hours, 40 minutes, and 7 seconds and ended on April 13, 1984, when Challenger landed safely on Runway 17 at Edwards Air Force Base, having completed 108 orbits.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0237510c-6650-4905-91aa-dcb490c685e4","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:53.340Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0b179744-eda6-4a6b-b01b-eae9b8c4ed73","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Hartsfield, a veteran of STS-4, and first-time flyers Pilot Michael L. Coats, and Mission Specialists R. Michael Mullane, Steven A. Hawley, and Judith A. Resnik, all from the 1978 class of astronauts, were assigned to Discovery's first flight. In May 1983, NASA announced the addition of Charles D. Walker, an employee of the McDonnell Douglas Corporation, to the crew, flying as the first commercial payload specialist.\n\n## Satellite Deployments\n\nDuring the six-day mission, the crew deployed a then-record three commercial satellites, tested an experimental solar array, and ran a commercial biotechnology experiment. Specifically, on the first day Mullane and Hawley deployed the SBS-4 satellite; on the second day in space, they deployed Leasat, the first satellite designed specifically to be launched from the shuttle; and on the third day, they deployed the Telstar satellite, completing the satellite delivery objectives of the mission.\n\n## Solar Array Testing\n\nResnik deployed the OAST-1 solar array to 70% of its length to conduct dynamic tests on the structure, and on the fourth day, she deployed the solar array to its full length and successfully retracted it, completing all objectives for that experiment.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0ff45462-38b4-44d1-b865-4678f54e62c2","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:04.152Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d98e87fe-24c4-4ecd-b4a2-89b46d6e10de","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mission launched from Kennedy Space Center on November 8, 1984, and landed just under eight days later on November 16, 1984.\n\n## Satellite Retrieval Operations\n\nSTS-51-A marked the first time a shuttle deployed two communications satellites, and retrieved from orbit two other communications satellites. Palapa B2 and Westar 6 had been deployed during the STS-41-B mission earlier in the year, but had been placed into improper orbits due to the malfunctioning of their kick motors; they were both safely recovered and returned to Earth during STS-51-A.\n\n## EVA Details - Allen and Gardner\n\nMission specialists Allen and Gardner performed an EVA, capturing the satellite with a device known as a \"Stinger\" (Apogee Capture Device (ACD)), which was inserted into the satellite's apogee motor nozzle by Allen.\n\n### Palapa B2 Recovery\nOn day five of the mission, Discovery rendezvoused with Palapa. The satellite's rotation was slowed to 1 RPM, and Gardner, operating from a position on the end of the Remote Manipulator System (RMS) (Canadarm), attempted unsuccessfully to grapple the satellite. Allen was able to manually maneuver the satellite into its cradle with Gardner's help, further aided by the Canadarm, which was operated by Fisher. The successful, improvised rescue effort took two hours.\n\n### Westar 6 Recovery\nGardner, using the same muscle-power technique Allen had used for Palapa B2's rescue, easily captured the satellite. With Allen's help, he placed it in a cradle in the cargo bay.\n\n## Crew\n\nThe crew of Commander Frederick H. \"Rick\" Hauck, Pilot David M. Walker, and Mission Specialists Joseph P. Allen, Anna L. Fisher, and Dale A. Brand | Third spaceflight |\n| Pilot | Robert L. Gibson | First spaceflight |\n| Mission Specialist 1 | Bruce McCandless II | First spaceflight |\n| Mission Specialist 2/Flight Engineer | Robert L. Stewart | First spaceflight |\n| Mission Specialist 3 | Ronald E. McNair | First spaceflight; only mission (lost in STS-51-L) |\n\n## Orbiter Specifications\n- **Vehicle:** Space Shuttle Challenger (OV-099)\n- **Launch Mass:** 113,603 kg\n- **Landing Mass:** 91,280 kg\n- **Payload Mass:** 12,815 kg\n- **Orbits Completed:** 128\n- **Distance Traveled:** 5,329,150 km\n\n## Orbital Parameters\n- **Apogee:** 317 km\n- **Perigee:** 307 km\n- **Inclination:** 28.50°\n- **Orbital Period:** 90.80 minutes\n\n## Satellite Deployments\nBoth communications satellites were Hughes HS-376-series satellites deployed approximately 8 hours after launch:\n- **Westar 6:** For Western Union (USA)\n- **Palapa B2:** For Indonesia\n\nBoth experienced Payload Assist Module malfunctions, achieving lower-than-planned orbits and were subsequently retrieved during STS-51-A in November 1984.\n\n## Extravehicular Activities (EVAs)\n\n### EVA 1 (February 7, 1984)\n- **Duration:** 5 hours, 55 minutes\n- **Crew:** McCandless and Stewart\n- **Key Achievement:** \"first untethered spacewalk, operating the Manned Maneuvering Unit (MMU) for the first time\"\n- **Maximum Distance:** McCandless reached 98 meters (322 feet) from the orbiter at 8:25 a.m. EST\n- **Activities:** Stewart tested the work station foot restraint at the end of the Remote Manipulator System (Canadarm)\n\n### EVA 2 (February 9, 1984)\n- **Duration:** 6 hours, 17 minutes\n- **Crew:** McCandless and Stewart\n- **Purpose:** Practice capture procedures for Solar Maximum Mission (SMM) satellite retrieval and repair, planned for STS-41-C\n\n## Scientific Experiments & Payloads\n- **SPAS-1:** West German-sponsored pallet/satellite (reflight from STS-7); remained in payload bay due to Canadarm electrical problem\n- **Get Away Special (GAS) canisters:** Five units\n- **Live specimens:** Six rats in middeck area\n- **Cameras:** Cinema-360 camera\n- **Materials processing:** Continuous Flow Electrophoresis System and Monodisperse Latex Reactor experiments\n- **Educational payload:** First high school-designed experiment (Brighton High School, Utah) on seed germination in zero gravity\n\n## Technical Issues\n\nDuring the mission, a malfunction occurred in the environmental systems. The \"supply and wastewater venting systems experienced below-freezing temperatures; subsequently, the supply water dump valve failed to open.\" Ice formed in the dump lines, and during re-entry, ice fragments detached and struck the left Orbital Maneuvering System pod, damaging three thermal protection tiles and causing minor burn-through. The damage proved minimal and posed no safety risk to crew or vehicle.\n\nPost-flight inspection revealed the dump line had ruptured from ice expansion and insulation was missing around the nozzles.\n\n## Mission Designation\nOriginally designated STS-11 internally; renumbered to STS-41-B following changes to the Space Shuttle flight numbering system after STS-9."}]},"uuid":"14b50732-653f-4283-923f-91de069ce9b6","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:34:40.033Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"bd6905fd-d902-41be-9970-d5a31a575fee","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here's a comprehensive overview of the key details:\n\n## The Disaster\n\nOn January 28, 1986, Space Shuttle Challenger broke apart 73 seconds into its flight, killing all seven crew members.\n\n## Crew Names\n\nThe seven crew members were Commander Francis R. \"Dick\" Scobee, Pilot Michael J. Smith, Mission Specialists Ellison S. Onizuka, Judith A. Resnik and Ronald E. McNair, and Payload Specialists Gregory B. Jarvis and S. Christa McAuliffe.\n\n## O-Ring Failure and Temperature Data\n\nThe Rogers Commission later determined the cause of the accident to have been the failure of the primary and secondary (backup) O-ring seals on Challenger's right Solid Rocket Booster (SRB).\n\nMorton Thiokol called a meeting the night before the launch to raise concerns over the forecast temperature in regards to the o-rings, with engineers issuing a recommendation \"not to launch below 53°F\", the previous lowest temperature of a launch, STS-51-C, a year earlier. The January 1985 launch of STS-51-C was the coldest Space Shuttle launch to date, with the air temperature at 62 °F (17 °C) at the time of launch, and the calculated O-ring temperature was 53 °F (12 °C).\n\n## Richard Feynman's Role\n\nDuring a televised hearing on February 11, Feynman demonstrated the loss of rubber's elasticity in cold temperatures using a glass of cold water and a piece of rubber, for which he received media attention. He threatened to remove his name from the report unless it included his personal observations on reliability, which appeared as Appendix F.\n\n## Rogers Commission Findings\n\nThe report, released and submitted to President Ronald Reagan on June 9, 1986, determined the cause of the disaster that took place 73 seconds after liftoff, and urged the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA) to improve and install new safety features on the shuttles and in its organizational handling of future missions.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a3bbc03e-f433-4430-9250-f8b86b8c2649","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:35:38.387Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"964e02e8-7839-4a88-99ae-1ece09442a7c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Jarvis (Hughes Space Communications; only mission)\n\n## Orbiter & Payload Specifications\n- **Orbiter:** Space Shuttle _Challenger_\n- **Launch Mass:** 1,217,990 kg (2,685,210 lb)\n- **Planned Landing Mass:** 90,584 kg (199,704 lb)\n- **Payload Mass:** 21,937 kg (48,363 lb)\n- **Planned Orbits:** 96 at 28.45° inclination\n\n## Primary Payload\n**TDRS-B** (Tracking and Data Relay Satellite-B) with Inertial Upper Stage booster\n\n## Secondary Payloads/Experiments\n- **SPARTAN-203:** Shuttle Pointed Autonomous Research Tool for Astronomy (Halley's Comet observation)\n- **CHAMP:** Comet Halley Active Monitoring Program\n- **FDE:** Fluid Dynamics Experiment\n- **PPE:** Phase Partitioning Experiment\n- **SSIP:** Three Shuttle Student Involvement Program experiments\n- **TISP:** Two Teacher in Space Project lessons\n\n## Structural Failure Details\n\n**Timing:** 73 seconds after launch (16:39:13 UTC)\n\n**Root Cause:** \"The failure of the primary and secondary (backup) O-ring seals on _Challenger_'s right Solid Rocket Booster (SRB)\" allowed hot gases to impinge on an aft strut, causing it to fail.\n\n**Sequence of Events:**\n1. O-ring failure on starboard SRB\n2. Flamethrower-like flare damaged one of two aft SRB attach struts\n3. Strut failed; booster pivoted on remaining attachment points\n4. Forward booster cylinder struck external tank inter-tank area\n5. Structural failure of Space Shuttle External Tank (ET)\n6. Rapid propellant combustion\n7. Orbiter experienced overwhelming aerodynamic forces during breakup\n\n**Distance Traveled:** 29 km (18 mi)\n**Actual Mission Duration:** 1 minute 13 seconds\n\n## Crew Fate & Survival Evidence\n\nThe crew cabin was located on the ocean floor on March 7, 1986, with remains recovered March 8.\n\n**Critical Finding:** Three of four recovered Personal Egress Air Packs (PEAPs) \"had been manually activated,\" indicating consciousness among at least some crew members during the initial breakup phase. However, these devices do not provide pressurized airflow; crew would have lost consciousness \"within several seconds\" from cabin depressurization.\n\n**Disputed Claims:** Media reports of recorded crew panic and on-board conversation during free fall are \"likely fabricated and no recording exists,\" as crew members did not wear individual voice recorders.\n\n## Temperature Context\n\nA Morton-Thiokol engineer quoted: \"we're only qualified to 40 degrees...what business does anyone even have thinking about 18 degrees, we're in no man's land, we're in a big grey area.\"\n\n## Rogers Commission & Recovery\n\n**Investigation:** President Ronald Reagan convened the Rogers Commission immediately after the failure.\n\n**Key Finding:** The launch approval proceeded despite the predicted ambient temperature of 27°F—well below component qualification limits.\n\n## Program Impact\n\n- **Flight Suspension:** 32 months\n- **Return to Flight:** September 1988 with STS-26\n- **Redesign Focus:** O-ring seals and other vehicle hazards\n\n**Note:** No cost estimates or specific redesign expenditures are provided in the source document."}]},"uuid":"e48413b7-c90c-4862-9f99-04267bcfcec3","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:35:58.733Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"ee92c0b1-76f6-4417-9f74-5d5e6fc22d4e","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

{"parentUuid":"b032dc5f-4843-4114-97cf-df5e7ecbf57d","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"123f0d48-caaf-4b8a-8aee-19b8828f0cc8","agentId":"a839aee13819dce56","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01Wz1cn1wjAx7Y1vnjptrjNV","type":"tool_result","content":"# Space Shuttle Challenger Disaster: Technical and Organizational Failures\n\n## O-Ring Technical Failure\n\nThe disaster resulted from a catastrophic seal failure in the right solid rocket booster's field joint. The O-rings—20-foot diameter Viton-rubber components with a 0.280-inch cross-section—were designed to contain hot, high-pressure gases. However, design flaws made them \"unacceptably sensitive to changes in temperature, dynamic loading, and the character of its materials,\" according to the Rogers Commission findings.\n\nThe joint design used a tang-and-clevis configuration with putty filling the gap between segments. Testing since 1977 had revealed that wide tolerances allowed O-rings to extrude from their seats rather than compress properly. Most critically, a 1977 test demonstrated up to 0.052 inches of joint rotation occurred during simulated launch pressure, which \"reduced the pressure on the O-rings, which weakened their seals.\"\n\n## Temperature Data and Previous Warning Signs\n\nOn January 28, 1986, overnight measurements showed the right SRB at just 8°F (−13°C)—far below the previous coldest launch on January 1985 at 53°F (12°C), when O-ring erosion was documented. The scheduled launch temperature was predicted to reach only 26°F (−3°C).\n\nPost-flight analysis from the 1985 STS-51-C mission revealed that \"cold temperatures caused a loss of flexibility in the O-rings that decreased their ability to seal the field joints,\" allowing hot gas bypass.\n\n## Morton Thiokol Engineer Warnings\n\nEngineers including Roger Boisjoly, Bob Ebeling, and Allan McDonald strongly opposed the launch. They argued they \"did not have enough data to determine whether the O-rings would seal at temperatures colder than 53°F.\" McDonald, the Space Shuttle SRM Project Director at Kennedy Space Center, refused to sign off on the launch decision, declaring he would not endorse it as the on-site representative.\n\nDespite their objections, Morton Thiokol management reversed position after management discussion, claiming the evidence was \"inconclusive.\" NASA SRB project manager Lawrence Mulloy then \"demanded to know if Morton Thiokol expected him to wait until April for warmer temperatures.\"\n\n## Feynman's Demonstration\n\nDuring Rogers Commission hearings on February 11, physicist Richard Feynman dramatically illustrated the problem by immersing a rubber sample in cold water, demonstrating how elasticity diminished in low temperatures—a demonstration that received significant media attention and became iconic in documenting the engineering failure.\n\n## Rogers Commission Findings\n\nThe commission's nine major recommendations included:\n\n1. Redesign of SRB field joints to prevent gas leakage\n2. Restructured management to prevent unsafe schedule pressure\n3. Establishment of an independent safety office reporting to the NASA administrator\n4. Improved crew escape capabilities during controlled flight\n5. Enhanced training and quality control\n6. Astronaut involvement in safety decisions\n7. Risk management reviews for all critical systems\n8. Increased reliability assessment\n9. Improved communication protocols\n\nThe report concluded that \"the safety culture and management structure at NASA were insufficient to properly report, analyze, and prevent flight issues.\"\n\n## Redesigned SRB Joint\n\nThe RSRM (Redesigned Solid Rocket Motor) incorporated a capture feature on the tang interior to prevent joint rotation—reducing rotation potential to 15% of disaster-level amounts. An additional O-ring sealed the space between capture feature and clevis. Heaters maintained consistent O-ring temperatures. These changes were successfully tested with intentional flaws in April and August 1988 before certification.\n\n## Crew Cabin Forensics\n\nThe crew compartment \"separated in one piece from the rest of the orbiter\" and traveled in a ballistic arc reaching 65,000 feet before falling at terminal velocity. Some crew members activated Personal Egress Air Packs, suggesting consciousness after initial breakup. However, the impact velocity of 207 mph produced deceleration forces of approximately 200 g—far exceeding human survivability limits.\n\nHuman remains were \"badly damaged from impact and submersion, and were not intact bodies.\" The exact cause of death could not be positively determined, though the impact forces alone would have been unsurvivable."}]},"uuid":"df8ec766-661b-4bbe-9cb3-200e298740e9","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:36:22.429Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b032dc5f-4843-4114-97cf-df5e7ecbf57d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Two months after its launch, on June 27, 1990, NASA announced that the telescope was flawed.\n\nThe outer edge of Hubble's main mirror was ground to the wrong specification—flattened by a depth of 2.2 microns (roughly equal to one-fiftieth the thickness of a human hair)—an error called spherical aberration. Because the outer edge of the mirror was flatter than specified, light landing on that outer edge had different focal points than the other parts of the mirror, creating multiple focal points which made its images blurry.\n\n## COSTAR Development and Installation\n\nThe Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement (COSTAR) is an optical correction instrument designed and built by NASA to correct the spherical aberration of the Hubble Space Telescope's primary mirror. It was flown via shuttle to the telescope in the servicing mission STS-61, on December 2, 1993, and successfully installed over a period of eleven days.\n\nCOSTAR consisted of five pairs of corrective mirrors placed in front of the Faint Object Camera, the Faint Object Spectrograph, and the Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph (GHRS) instruments. The resulting images confirmed that the COSTAR had corrected the spherical aberration in the primary mirror.\n\n## Five Servicing Missions\n\n### Servicing Mission 1 (SM1) - December 1993 (STS-61)\n\nAstronauts installed the Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement (COSTAR) unit in place of the High Speed Photometer. The WFPC was replaced with the WFPC2, which came with internal corrective optics, and Hubble's solar arrays and gyroscopes were upgraded to improve the telescope's tracking ability. The solar arrays and their drive electronics were also replaced, as well as four gyroscopes in the telescope pointing system, two electrical control units and other electrical components, and two magnetometers. The onboard computers were upgraded with added coprocessors, and Hubble's orbit was boosted.\n\n### Servicing Mission 2 (SM2) - February 1997 (STS-82)\n\nDiscovery launched on STS-82 in February 1997, with two new instruments for Hubble. The Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph (STIS) and Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer (NICMOS) replaced Hubble's GHRS and FOS. The swap expanded the telescope's vision into near-infrared wavelengths.\n\n### Servicing Mission 3A and 3B - December 1999 and March 2002\n\nWhat was originally conceived as a mission of preventive maintenance turned more urgent on Nov. 13, 1999, when the fourth of Hubble's six gyros failed. Hubble required at least three of its stabilizing gyros to conduct science at that time. Hubble entered a state of dormancy called safe mode while the telescope awaited repairs.\n\nServicing Mission 3B flown by Columbia in March 2002 saw the installation of a new instrument, with the FOC (which was the last of the original instruments) being replaced by the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS). The mission also revived NICMOS by installing a closed-cycle cooler and replaced the solar arrays for the second time, providing 30 percent more power.\n\n### Servicing Mission 4 (SM4) - May 2009 (STS-125)\n\nThe Hubble Space Telescope was reborn with Servicing Mission 4 (SM4), the fifth and final servicing of the orbiting observatory. During SM4, astronauts installed two new scientific instruments – the Cosmic Origins Spectrograph (COS) and Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3). Two failed instruments, the Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph (STIS) and the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS), were brought back to life by the first ever on-orbit repairs.\n\nAstronauts removed COSTAR in May 2009 during Servicing Mission 4 to make room to install the Cosmic Origins Spectrograph. To prolong Hubble's life, astronauts installed new batteries, new gyroscopes, a new science computer, a refurbished fine guidance sensor, and new insulation on three electronics bays.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"df1e9cd4-9dcb-4a87-895b-c3a77abce5df","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:36:44.292Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"32869552-3f95-4273-ac28-c6b627683759","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

COBE was launched into a 900-km-high polar Earth orbit in November 1989 and, over the next four years, observed the spectral and spatial properties of the cosmic microwave background (CMB) over the whole sky.\n\n## The Scientists and Nobel Prize\n\nThe Nobel Prize in Physics for 2006 is awarded to John C. Mather and George F. Smoot for their discovery of the basic form of the cosmic microwave background radiation as well as its small variations in different directions. The very detailed observations that the Laureates have carried out from the COBE satellite have played a major role in the development of modern cosmology into a precise science.\n\n## Key Discoveries\n\n### Blackbody Spectrum\nJohn Mather was in charge of one of the instruments on board, which was used to investigate the blackbody spectrum of the background radiation. The wavelength distribution of the cosmic microwave background radiation, measured by COBE, corresponds to a perfect blackbody spectrum. The wavelengths of the microwave background are found in the millimetre range, and this particular spectrum corresponds to a temperature of 2.7 degrees above absolute zero.\n\n### CMB Anisotropy\nGeorge Smoot was in charge of the other determinative instrument, which was to look for small variations of the background radiation in different directions. The variations are minuscule – in the range of a hundred-thousandth of a degree.\n\n## Scientific Significance\n\nCOBE's measurements provided two key pieces of evidence that supported the Big Bang theory of the universe: that the CMB has a near-perfect black-body spectrum, and that it has very faint anisotropies. According to the Nobel Prize committee, \"the COBE project can also be regarded as the starting point for cosmology as a precision science\".\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4ea0305f-5ec3-4352-a74f-290d3ea33275","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:37:19.303Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e8325483-7998-4ed2-9777-8ac63c01bbc2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

DIRBE (Diffuse Infrared Background Experiment)**\n- Principal Investigator: Michael G. Venus Radar Mapper\",\"url\":\"https://www.aerospaceguide.net/spaceprobe/magellanspacecraft.html\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the Magellan spacecraft Venus radar mapper mission:\n\n## Launch and Deployment\n\nMagellan was launched on May 4, 1989, at 18:46:59 UTC by NASA from KSC Launch Complex 39B at the Kennedy Space Center in Florida, aboard Space Shuttle Atlantis during mission STS-30. The Magellan/Venus radar mapper spacecraft and attached Inertial Upper Stage (IUS) was deployed six hours, 14 minutes into flight. The Magellan probe was the first interplanetary mission to be launched from the Space Shuttle, the first one to use the Inertial Upper Stage booster, and the first spacecraft to test aerobraking as a method for circularizing its orbit.\n\n## Mission Objectives\n\nIts mission objectives were to map the surface of Venus by using synthetic-aperture radar and to measure the planetary gravitational field. Magellan was designed to use a Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR) to map 70% of the Venusian surface down to a resolution of 390 to 985 feet (120 to 300 meters).\n\n## Journey and Orbit Insertion\n\nOn August 10, 1990, Magellan encountered Venus and began the orbital insertion maneuver which placed the spacecraft into a three-hour, nine minute, elliptical orbit that brought the spacecraft 295-kilometers from the surface at about 10 degrees North during the periapsis and out to 7762-kilometers during apoapsis.\n\n## Mapping Results and Discoveries\n\nMagellan completed its first 243-day cycle (i.e., the time it took for Venus to rotate once under Magellan's orbit) of radar mapping on May 15, 1991, providing the first clear views of 83.7% of the surface. The spacecraft remained healthy and NASA extended its mission to conduct an additional five 243-day imaging cycles, which in aggregate increased the area of the planet mapped to 98%.\n\nBeginning Sept. 15, 1990, the spacecraft began returning high-quality radar images of the Venusian terrain that showed evidence of volcanism, tectonic movement, turbulent surface winds, miles (kilometers) of lava channels, and pancake-shaped domes. Magellan found that at least 85% of the Venusian surface is covered with volcanic flows. It was the first spacecraft to image the entire surface of Venus and made several discoveries about the planet.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0edd0879-aca1-498c-9845-12971abf8e1b","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:37:51.884Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d0a967df-7163-4eb1-9845-b47981cf2e1b","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

AmericaSpace\",\"url\":\"https://www.americaspace.com/2023/10/18/happiness-is-remembering-sts-34-and-galileo-otd-in-1989/\"},{\"title\":\"Welcome to the Galileo Orbiter Archive Page\",\"url\":\"https://pds-atmospheres.nmsu.edu/data_and_services/atmospheres_data/Galileo/galileo_orbiter.html\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the Galileo spacecraft mission:\n\n## Launch Details\n\nThe Galileo spacecraft was delivered into Earth orbit on October 18, 1989, by Space Shuttle Atlantis, during STS-34. The five-person crew of Commander Donald E. Williams, Pilot Michael J. McCulley, and Mission Specialists Shannon W. Lucid, Franklin R. Chang-Díaz, and Ellen S. Baker deployed the spacecraft, managed by NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory. Atlantis lifted off at 16:53:40 UTC on October 18, and went into a 343-kilometer (213 mi) orbit.\n\n## Spacecraft Mass and Configuration\n\nAt launch, the orbiter and probe together had a mass of 2,562 kg (5,648 lb) and stood 6.15 m (20.2 ft) tall. The Galileo spacecraft consisted of an orbiter and an atmospheric entry probe.\n\n## Mission Timeline\n\nGalileo arrived at Jupiter on December 7, 1995, after gravitational assist flybys of Venus and Earth, and became the first spacecraft to orbit an outer planet. The spacecraft's path took it first to Venus on Feb. 10, 1990, back to Earth on Dec. 8, 1990, and again on Dec. 8, 1992. Along the way, Galileo also passed by and imaged the main belt asteroids Gaspra and Ida and observed the crash of Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9 onto Jupiter.\n\n## Scientific Instruments\n\nThere were eleven subsystems and nine scientific instruments on the orbiter, including remote sensing instruments such as the Photopolarimeter Radiometer, Ultraviolet Spectrometer, Near-Infrared Mapping Spectrometer, Solid State Imaging Camera, Energetic Particles Detector, Dust Detector Subsystem, Plasma Detector, Plasma Wave Subsystem, Magnetometer, and Heavy Ion Counter.\n\n## Major Discoveries\n\nGalileo was the first mission to measure Jupiter's atmosphere directly with a descent probe and the first to conduct long-term observations of the jovian system from orbit. It found evidence of subsurface liquid layers of saltwater on Europa, Ganymede and Callisto and it examined a diversity of volcanic activity on Io. Galileo is the first spacecraft to fly by an asteroid and the first to discover a moon of an asteroid.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4529c617-25c0-4b6e-8ee6-db99c6f3cbcd","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:38:07.819Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"860c35d1-0ce6-4f5b-9983-189437f6788d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Due to the 28 January 1986 loss of Challenger, the launch was delayed until 6 October 1990 aboard Discovery (mission STS-41). Discovery lifted off on October 6 1990 at 7:47:16 a.m. EDT, with liftoff occurring 12 minutes after a two-and-a-half-hour launch window opened.\n\nSix hours after Discovery's launch, Ulysses was deployed from the payload bay. From there, it was propelled on a trajectory to Jupiter by a combination of solid rocket motors consisting of a two-stage Boeing IUS (Inertial Upper Stage), plus a McDonnell Douglas PAM-S (Payload Assist Module-Special).\n\n## Orbit and Trajectory\n\nEscape velocity was about 10 miles per second (15.4 kilometers per second), higher than had been achieved by either of the Voyagers or Pioneers, and the fastest velocity ever achieved by a human-made object at the time. To reach this out-of-ecliptic trajectory, Ulysses was sent out to the planet Jupiter, whose immense gravity was used to deflect the spacecraft in a slingshot fashion into a highly inclined orbit (80º inclined to the ecliptic).\n\nAfter a midcourse correction July 8, 1991, Ulysses passed within about 235,000 miles (378,400 kilometers) of Jupiter at 12:02 UT Feb. 8, 1992, becoming the fifth spacecraft to reach Jupiter.\n\n## Mission Discoveries\n\nUlysses flew past the south pole of the Sun on Sept. 13, 1994, and over the Sun's north pole in 1995, at a time of minimum solar activity. It flew over the poles once again in 2000–01, this time during maximum solar activity, and again in 2006–08, during another solar minimum but with the polarity of the Sun's magnetic field reversed.\n\nAmong Ulysses' discoveries was that the solar wind speed did not increase continuously toward the poles but rather at high latitudes leveled off at 750 km (450 miles) per second. Three times during its mission, the spacecraft unexpectedly passed through comet tails — the first time in May 1996 (comet C/1996 B2 Hyakutake), the second time in 1999 (comet C/1999 T1 McNaught-Hartley), and the third time in 2007 (comet C/2006 P1 McNaught).\n\nMission operations continued at reduced capacity until loss of contact on June 30, 2009, more than 18.5 years after launch.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"487aaa9c-eed8-48f9-9d1b-a2051a3e91ec","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:38:24.492Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0b3f6c70-cf4e-44d9-8802-02d2a38f7d07","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It was the heaviest astrophysical payload ever flown at that time at 16,300 kilograms (35,900 lb).\n\n## Instruments\n\nCGRO carried a complement of four instruments that covered an unprecedented six orders of the electromagnetic spectrum, from 20 keV to 30 GeV. The instruments included the Burst and Transient Source Experiment (BATSE); the Imaging Compton Telescope (COMPTEL); the Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope (EGRET); and the Oriented Scintillation Spectrometer Telescope (OSSEE).\n\n## Gamma Ray Bursts\n\nThe Burst and Transient Source Experiment (BATSE) searched the sky for gamma-ray bursts (20 to >600 keV) and conducted full-sky surveys for long-lived sources. Bursts were typically detected at rates of roughly one per day over the 9-year CGRO mission.\n\n## Mission Duration\n\nThe CGRO was a space observatory detecting photons with energies from 20 keV to 30 GeV, in Earth orbit from 1991 to 2000. The observatory featured four main telescopes in one spacecraft, covering X-rays and gamma rays, including various specialized sub-instruments and detectors.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9c7eb7fa-6144-43c8-a2e9-7c624ac4cbde","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:38:35.160Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7ffb0234-ba3c-4d6f-ae28-fbc4107aa66b","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The 5,900-kilogram (13,000 lb) satellite was deployed from Space Shuttle Discovery during the STS-48 mission on 15 September 1991.\n\n## Satellite Specifications\n\nThe Upper Atmosphere Research Satellite (UARS) was a NASA-operated orbital observatory whose mission was to study the Earth's atmosphere, particularly the protective ozone layer. It entered Earth orbit at an operational altitude of 600 kilometers (370 mi), with an orbital inclination of 57 degrees.\n\n## Scientific Instruments\n\nUARS had ten sensing and measuring devices: Cryogenic Limb Array Etalon Spectrometer (CLAES); Improved Stratospheric and Mesospheric Sounder (ISAMS); Microwave Limb Sounder (MLS); Halogen Occultation Experiment (HALOE); High Resolution Doppler Imager (HRDI); Wind Imaging Interferometer (WINDII); Solar Ultraviolet Spectral Irradiance Monitor (SUSIM); Solar/Stellar Irradiance Comparison Experiment (SOLSTICE); Particle Environment Monitor (PEM) and Active Cavity Radiometer Irradiance Monitor (ACRIM II).\n\n## Instruments for Ozone Depletion Studies\n\nUARS's 10 instruments were designed to operate as a single experiment, capable of providing atmospheric scientists with an opportunity to make simultaneous measurements of all factors affecting ozone depletion. The Cryogenic Limb Array Etalon Spectrometer examined concentrations of nitrogen and chlorine, ozone, water vapour, and methane, whilst the Improved Stratospheric and Mesospheric Sounder also monitored carbon dioxide, nitrous oxide, nitric acid, and carbon monoxide. Meanwhile, the Microwave Limb Sounder provided the first \"global\" data set on chlorine monoxide (the key intermediate compound in the ozone-destruction cycle), and the Halogen Occultation Experiment observed vertical distributions of hydrofluoric acid and members of the nitrogen family.\n\n## Mission Success and Longevity\n\nThe original mission duration was to be only three years, but was extended several times. When the mission finally ended in June 2005 due to funding cuts, 14 years after the satellite's launch, six of its ten instruments were still operational.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"e11a4ff6-a922-4c4c-8bca-85e615528a3b","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:38:49.687Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b6ba4053-9e38-4aaa-ba05-dc9ada2b06f1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It was designed to study the surface, atmosphere, climate and magnetic field of Mars.\n\n## Mass and Dimensions\n\nThe spacecraft had a mass of 1,018 kilograms. It had the dimensions of an oversized refrigerator (2.1 x 1.5 x 1.1m).\n\n## Scientific Instruments\n\nSeven scientific instruments were included. The spacecraft carried a deployable high-gain antenna, a magnetometer, gamma-ray spectrometer, and thermal emission spectrometer.\n\n## Communication Loss and Failure\n\nOn August 21, 1993, during the interplanetary cruise phase, communication with Mars Observer was lost, three days prior to its scheduled orbital insertion around Mars. Attempts to re-establish communications with the spacecraft were unsuccessful.\n\n## Cause of Failure\n\nOn January 4, 1994, an independent investigation board from the Naval Research Laboratory announced their findings: the most probable cause in the loss of communication was a rupture of the fuel pressurization tank in the spacecraft's propulsion system. The board concluded that the most probable cause was the unintended mixing of nitrogen tetroxide (NTO) and monomethyl hydrazine (MMH) in the titanium tubing, believed to have been enabled by significant NTO migration through check valves during the eleven-month cruise phase from Earth to Mars.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9c804dcd-8d59-4ead-9413-975e3049553b","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:39:00.791Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5c954cfd-05fc-4b04-8dbb-b6eb570d6dbb","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA - NSSDCA - Spacecraft - Details\",\"url\":\"https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/spacecraft/display.action?id=1994-004A\"},{\"title\":\"Clementine Spacecraft - an overview | ScienceDirect Topics\",\"url\":\"https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/earth-and-planetary-sciences/clementine-spacecraft\"},{\"title\":\"A CLEMENTINE COLLECTI ON MOON GL O W N a v a l Resear ch Laboratory\",\"url\":\"https://www.nrl.navy.mil/Portals/38/PDF%20Files/Clementine_2019_BOOKLET.pdf?ver=xKUITdTJYGGDWP6ohsSYoA%3D%3D\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the Clementine spacecraft's 1994 lunar mission:\n\n## Launch Details\n\nClementine was launched on January 25, 1994, from Space Launch Complex 4 West at Vandenberg Air Force Base, California, using a Titan II launch vehicle.\n\n## Mission Objectives and Overview\n\nIt was designed to test spacecraft components during extended exposure to space and to study the Moon and an asteroid. Clementine was a joint project between the Ballistic Missile Defense Organization (BMDO, nee the Strategic Defense Initiative Organization, or SDIO) and NASA. The objective of the mission was to test sensors and spacecraft components under extended exposure to the space environment and to make scientific observations of the Moon and the near-Earth asteroid 1620 Geographos.\n\n## Spacecraft Mass\n\nMass: 227 kg\n\n## Orbit Details\n\nClementine successfully entered an elliptical polar orbit (about 270 × 1,830 miles or 430 × 2,950 kilometers) around the Moon on Feb. 19, 1994, with a period of five days. The first part consisted of a five hour elliptical polar orbit with a periapsis of about 400 km at 30 degrees south latitude and an apoapsis of 8300 km. Each orbit consisted of an 80 minute lunar mapping phase near periapsis and 139 minutes of downlink at apoapsis.\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory (LLNL) in Livermore, California, provided the nine science instruments, including lightweight imaging cameras and ranging sensors. The lunar observations made included imaging at various wavelengths in the visible as well as in ultraviolet and infrared, laser ranging altimetry, gravimetry, and charged particle measurements.\n\n## Water Ice Evidence\n\nThe probe found evidence of ice in the bottom of a permanently shadowed crater at the Moon's south pole. Data from a radar instrument indicated that large quantities of water ice may lie in permanently shadowed craters at lunar south pole, while other polar regions may remain in near permanent sunlight.\n\n## Mission Results\n\nIn the following two months, it transmitted about 1.6 million digital images of the lunar surface, many of them with resolutions down to about 330-660 feet (100-200 meters). The mission succeeded in its lunar objectives, but a malfunction forced the asteroid flyby to be canceled.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"45032bee-f0ab-465d-ae63-26bfc53c9c82","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:39:16.662Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1c82ed43-c1e3-44f8-b861-ce9ca69d9340","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mission launched on February 3, 1995, at 12:22:04 a.m. EST and landed on February 11, 1995, at 6:50:19 a.m. EST, lasting 8 days, 6 hours, 28 minutes, 15 seconds.\n\n## Crew Members\n\nThe six-person crew included Commander James Wetherbee, Pilot Eileen Collins – the first woman to pilot a space shuttle mission – Payload Commander Bernard Harris, and Mission Specialists Michael Foale, Janice Voss, and Vladimir Titov.\n\n## The Rendezvous\n\nWith Wetherbee manually controlling the orbiter, Discovery was flown to 11 meters (36 ft) from the Russian space station on February 6, 1995 at 19:23:20 UTC. Titov, who had previously lived on Mir for more than a year, communicated excitedly with the three cosmonauts aboard the space station: Mir 17 Commander Alexander Viktorenko; Flight Engineer Yelena Kondakova; and Valery Polyakov, a physician who had broken Titov's record for extended time in space.\n\n## Historic Achievements\n\nThe spacewalk conducted during the mission included the first African American and the first British born astronauts to walk in space. Atlantis returned to Earth on July 7 with a crew of eight.\n\n## Historic Achievements\n\nSTS-71 marked several key achievements: it was the first Shuttle docking with a space station, the first crew exchange between a Shuttle and a station, and the 100th crewed space launch by the United States. SOHO was placed at a distance of 932,000 miles (1.5 million kilometers) from Earth in an elliptical Lissajous orbit around the L1 libration point.\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nThe ESA-sponsored Solar and Heliospheric Observatory (SOHO) carries 12 scientific instruments to study the solar atmosphere, helioseismology, and the solar wind. SOHO is a cooperative international project between ESA and NASA, with ESA leading the mission.\n\n## Spacecraft Details\n\nSOHO has a gross mass of 1,850 kg (4,070 lb). The spacecraft has a height of 3.65 m (11.97 ft) and a span of 9.50 m (31.10 ft).\n\n## Instruments and Discoveries\n\nThe instruments CDS, EIT, LASCO, SUMER, SWAN, and UVCS are used for solar atmosphere remote sensing; CELIAS and COSTEP are used for \"in situ\" solar wind observations; and GOLF, MDI, and VIRGO are used for helioseismology.\n\nSOHO has made many new discoveries, including more than 5,000 comets. Information from the mission has allowed scientists to learn more about the Sun's internal structure and dynamics, the chromosphere, the corona, and solar particles.\n\n## Mission Success\n\nThough its mission was scheduled to run until only 1998, it has continued collecting data, and SOHO continues to operate after 30 years in space; the mission has been extended until September of 2026.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a999f1b7-ccc7-457b-88df-65a4aecfc5e1","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:39:55.154Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5ba41cf7-57ac-48e5-81da-db333f5d384c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

{"parentUuid":"b45bd5a2-64be-4652-88a5-cc851f8d9086","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"271c1fc1-4de5-49e3-abbc-ab0155b76cec","agentId":"a839aee13819dce56","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01CSiGDUpQ6BVAGYu3bRdY66","type":"tool_result","content":"# Magellan Spacecraft: Key Details\n\n## Launch Information\n**Date/Time:** May 4, 1989, 18:46:59 UTC from Kennedy Space Center LC-39B aboard Space Shuttle Atlantis (STS-30)\n**Deployment:** May 5, 1989, 01:06:00 UTC with Inertial Upper Stage booster\n\n## Spacecraft Specifications\n- **Dry Mass:** 1,035 kg (2,282 lb)\n- **Total Launch Mass:** 3,445 kg (7,595 lb)\n- **Dimensions:** 6.4 meters tall, 4.6 meters diameter\n- **Power:** ~1,030 watts (solar arrays initially supplied 1,200 watts)\n- **Manufacturer:** Martin Marietta (spacecraft bus); Hughes Aircraft (radar system)\n\n## Venus Orbital Parameters\n- **Arrival:** August 10, 1990\n- **Orbital Period:** 3.26 hours (elliptical initially)\n- **Periapsis Altitude:** 295 km (later circularized to 180 km)\n- **Apoapsis Altitude:** 7,762 km (later circularized to 540 km)\n- **Inclination:** 85.5°\n\n## Primary Instrument: Radar System (RDRS)\nThe system operated in three modes using \"a 3.7-meter parabolic, high-gain antenna\":\n- **Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR):** 12.6-centimeter wavelength microwave pulses\n- **Altimetry (ALT):** Elevation measurements with 50 km spatial and 100 m vertical resolution\n- **Radiometry (RAD):** Surface temperature characterization\n\n**Data Rate:** 750 kilobits/second to tape recorder\n\n## Mission Phases & Coverage\n\n| Cycle | Dates | Coverage | Purpose |\n|-------|-------|----------|---------|\n| 1 | Sept 15, 1990–May 15, 1991 | 83.7% | Primary mapping (left-looking) |\n| 2 | May 16, 1991–Jan 14, 1992 | 54.5% additional | Southern hemisphere (right-looking) |\n| 3 | Jan 15–Sept 13, 1992 | 21.3% stereo | 3D imagery, totaled 98% coverage |\n| 4 | Sept 14, 1992–May 23, 1993 | N/A | Gravity field measurement |\n| 5 | May 24, 1993–Aug 29, 1994 | 94% high-resolution | Aerobraking + gravimetry |\n| 6 | Apr 16–Oct 13, 1994 | High-res gravity | Final operations + Windmill experiment |\n\n## Key Scientific Discoveries\n- Predominantly volcanic surface with vast lava plains\n- \"Fields of small lava domes, and large shield volcanoes are common\"\n- Few impact craters suggesting geologically young surface (<800 million years old)\n- \"Lava Compton Gamma Ray Observatory: Complete Mission Details\n\n## Launch Information\n**Date/Time:** April 5, 1991, 14:22:45 UTC  \n**Spacecraft:** Space Shuttle Atlantis (STS-37)  \n**Launch Site:** Kennedy Space Center LC-39B  \n**Mass:** 16,329 kilograms (35,999 lb)\n\n## Orbital Parameters\n- **Altitude:** 362-457 km (perigee-apogee)\n- **Inclination:** 28.46°\n- **Period:** 91.59 minutes\n- **Classification:** Low Earth orbit (geocentric)\n- **Eccentricity:** 0.006998\n\n## Four Main Instruments\n\n**BATSE (Burst and Transient Source Experiment)**\n- Range: 20 keV to >8 MeV\n- Eight detector modules at satellite corners\n- Configuration: NaI(Tl) Large Area Detectors with plastic scintillator anticoincidence shielding\n- Detected approximately one gamma-ray burst daily\n\n**OSSE (Oriented Scintillation Spectrometer Experiment)**\n- Range: 0.05–10 MeV\n- Four individually-pointable detector modules\n- Central NaI(Tl) crystal (12-inch diameter, 4-inch thick)\n- CsI(Na) backing crystal with phoswich design\n- Slew capability: ~2°/second\n\n**COMPTEL (Imaging Compton Telescope)**\n- Range: 0.75–30 MeV\n- One steradian field of view\n- Compton scattering detection in front/rear scintillators\n- Angular resolution: within one degree\n- Energy resolution: five percent at higher energies\n\n**EGRET (Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope)**\n- Range: 20 MeV–30 GeV\n- Electron-positron pair production detection\n- Position accuracy: fraction of a degree\n- Energy accuracy: within 15%\n\n## Deployment and EVA\nAstronaut Jay Apt conducted the first EVA of STS-37, photographed in the Space Shuttle bay while working with the partially-deployed observatory still attached to the robotic arm.\n\n## Key Discoveries\n- \"EGRET instrument conducted the first all sky survey above 100 MeV. Using four years of data it discovered 271 sources, 170 of which were unidentified.\"\n- Demonstrated gamma-ray burst origins in distant galaxies rather than the Milky Way\n- Identified two GRB classifications: short duration (<2 seconds) and long duration (>2 seconds)\n- Detected terrestrial gamma-ray sources from thunderclouds (1994)\n- Completed 26Al isotope sky map via COMPTEL\n\n## GRB 990123 Discovery\nJanuary 23, 1999 event provided the first optical afterglow observation during prompt emission, establishing redshift of 1.6 and distance of 3.2 Gpc—converting approximately two solar masses into energy.\n\n## Mission Operations\n- **Duration:** 9 years, 2 months (1991–2000)\n- **Orbital Reboosts:** \n  - October 1993: 340 km → 450 km\n  - June 1997: 440 km → 515 km\n- **Data Recorder Failure:** 1992 (mitigated by additional ground station)\n\n## Deorbit and End of Mission\n- **Gyroscope Failure:** December 1999 (one of three)\n- **Deorbit Date:** June 4, 2000, 23:29:55 UTC\n- **Debris:** \"six 1,800-pound aluminum I-beams and parts made of titanium, including more than 5,000 bolts\" fell into Pacific Ocean\n- **Historic First:** NASA's first intentional controlled satellite deorbit\n\n## Institutional Details\n- **Cost:** $617 million\n- **Manufacturer:** TRW Inc. gravity and atmosphere measurements\n8. **Mars Balloon Relay (MBR)** - relay capability (planned augmentation)\n\n## Communication Systems\n- **High-gain antenna:** 1.5-meter parabolic dish on 6-meter boom\n- **X-band transponders:** Two NASA transponders\n- **Downlink:** 10.66 kilobytes/second maximum\n- **Uplink:** 62.5 bytes/second maximum\n- **Low/medium-gain antennas:** Six low-gain, one medium-gain (backup/cruise phase)\n\n## Planned Orbital Parameters\n- **Semi-major axis:** 3,766.159 km\n- **Eccentricity:** 0.004049\n- **Inclination:** 92.869° (polar, sun-synchronous)\n- **Orbital period:** Approximately 118 minutes\n- **Orbital speed:** ~3.4 km/s\n- **Spacecraft velocity at Mars arrival:** 5.28 km/s (relative to Mars)\n\n## Mission Timeline\n**Launch:** September 25, 1992\n**Scheduled Mars Orbital Insertion:** August 24, 1993\n**Communication Loss:** August 21, 1993, 01:00 UTC (3 days before MOI)\n**Closest approach to Mars:** August 24, 1993 (unrealized)\n\n## Mission Objectives\n- Determine global elemental and mineralogical surface character\n- Define planetary topography and gravitational field\n- Establish nature of Martian magnetic field\n- Determine temporal and spatial distribution of volatiles and dust\n- Explore atmospheric structure and circulation patterns\n- Expected data yield: Over 75 gigabytes during primary mission\n\n## Failure Investigation Findings\n\n**Probable Cause:** \"Rupture of the fuel (monomethyl hydrazine (MMH)) pressurization side of the spacecraft's propulsion system, resulting in a pressurized leak of both helium gas and liquid MMH\"\n\n**Failure Mechanism:** Inadvertent mixing and reaction of nitrogen tetroxide and MMH within titanium pressurization tubing during helium tank pressurization. This caused tubing rupture, releasing gases that forced the spacecraft into catastrophic spin, damaging critical electrical circuits.\n\n**Secondary Effects:** High spin rate induced \"contingency mode,\" interrupting stored command sequences and preventing transmitter activation. Improper solar array orientation caused battery discharge.\n\n**Root Issue:** Over-reliance on heritage spacecraft systems not optimized for Mars Observer's specific operational requirements.\n\n## Program Cost\n**Total Program Cost:** $813 million\n\n## Manufacturer\n**Primary Contractor:** Martin Marietta\n**Spacecraft Bus Developer:** General Electric Astro Space (based on RCA AS-4000 design)\n\n## Post-Mission Legacy\nDespite mission failure, seven scientific instruments were successfully incorporated into subsequent Mars missions:\n- **Mars Global Surveyor** (1996): MOC, MOLA, TES, MAG/ER, RS, MBR\n- **Mars Climate Orbiter** (1998): PMIRR\n- **2001 Mars Odyssey** (2001): GRS\n- **Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter** (2005): PMIRR derivative"}]},"uuid":"eb47f0f3-b890-4890-acb6-8aeb05b4a931","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:41:44.711Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6918f3a6-be9e-4939-85c6-23a456562eea","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

This prevented the planned asteroid flyby of 1620 Geographos.
