The spacecraft \"orbits L1 once every six months, while L1 itself orbits the Sun every 12 months.\"\n\n**Perigee:** 206,448 km\n**Apogee:** 668,672 km\n\n## The 12 Instruments\n\n1. **CDS** - Measures density, temperature, flows in corona\n2. **CELIAS** - Studies ion composition of solar wind\n3. **COSTEP** - Analyzes ion/electron composition\n4. **EIT** - Extreme ultraviolet imaging of coronal structure\n5. **ERNE** - Energetic particle analysis\n6. **GOLF** - Measures solar disk velocity variations\n7. **LASCO** - Large angle spectrometric coronagraph\n8. **MDI** - Velocity/magnetic field measurement (superseded 2011)\n9. **SUMER** - Solar UV measurement of emitted radiation\n10. **SWAN** - Solar wind anisotropies via hydrogen wavelengths\n11. **UVCS** - Ultraviolet coronagraph spectrometer\n12. **VIRGO** - Solar irradiance and gravity oscillations\n\n## Mission Performance\n\n**Original Duration:** 3 years (planned)\n**Actual Duration:** 30+ years (extended to September 2026)\n**Operations Start:** May 1996\n\n**Data Transmission:** \"200 kbit/s continuous data stream\" via NASA Deep Space Network\n\n### Comet Discoveries\nSOHO has discovered \"more than 5,000 comets\" through its LASCO instrument, with \"an average discovery rate of one every 2.59 days\" as of 2014. Notable milestones include the 5,000th comet on March 25, 2024.\n\n### Space Weather Application\nThe mission provides \"near-real-time solar data\" used to predict coronal mass ejection arrival times, protecting \"electrical grids and satellites from their damaging effects.\"\n\n## Critical 1998 Incident\n\n**Timeline:** June 24-25, 1998\n- Lost sun lock during gyroscope calibration\n- Entered Emergency Sun Reacquisition mode\n- All contact lost by 04:43 UTC on June 25\n- Located via radar (Arecibo/Goldstone) on July 23\n- Carrier signal detected August 3\n- Full recovery completed by October 24, 1998\n\n**Recovery Innovation:** ESA developed \"gyroless operations mode\" implemented February 1, 1999, using reaction wheels as virtual gyroscopes—\"the first three-axis-stabilized spacecraft to use its reaction wheels\" this way.\n\n## Notable Design Features\nThe spacecraft avoids exact L1 placement to prevent \"radio interference generated by the Sun\" and because such positioning \"would not be a stable orbit.\""}]},"uuid":"842351b5-c7f3-40ff-a81d-1625e675ceb1","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:43:06.597Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f98e2bc8-b277-4745-ba0d-adcbe216694a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Most of the galaxies were so faint – four billion times fainter than the human eye can see – that they had never been observed before, even by the largest telescopes. No image has revolutionized the way we understand the universe as much as the Hubble Deep Field.\n\n## Dark Energy and Accelerating Universe\n\nHubble helped to make observations with higher and higher precision, leading to one of its most impactful contributions – realizing the Universe's expansion is actually accelerating. Astronomers were surprised when two independent teams discovered this acceleration in 1998. Hubble found that supernovae were dimmer than they should have been, which meant that their host galaxies were farther away than expected. And that only made sense if the universe's expansion was accelerating.\n\nMany scientists believe an invisible force, called \"dark energy,\" causes this acceleration.\n\n## Age of the Universe and Hubble Constant\n\nThe Hubble Constant puts the age of the universe at about 13.8 billion years old. After more than three decades of observations, teams using Hubble's extraordinary capabilities have measured the expansion rate to a precision of just over 1%, about eight times more precise than they originally anticipated!\n\nBefore Hubble's launch and its refinement of the Hubble Constant, the age of the universe was estimated at anywhere from 9.7 billion years to 19.5 billion years.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"86d8b728-34f8-4e84-b66f-3fdb82464955","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:43:20.038Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"fcf18e25-b48d-4b77-92ce-a61bdd9ac888","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here's a summary of the key details:\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nSTS-61 was NASA's first Hubble Space Telescope servicing mission, and the fifth flight of the Space Shuttle Endeavour. The mission launched on December 2, 1993, from Kennedy Space Center (KSC) in Florida.\n\n## Crew\n\nThe crew included Richard O. Covey (Commander), Kenneth D. Bowersox (Pilot), Kathryn C. Thornton, Claude Nicollier of the European Space Agency, Jeffrey A. Hoffman, F. Story Musgrave, and Thomas D. Akers.\n\n## EVA Operations\n\nSTS-61 lasted almost 11 days, and crew members made five spacewalks (extravehicular activities (EVAs)), an all-time record. During a record five back-to-back space walks totaling 35 hours and 28 minutes, two teams of astronauts completed the first servicing of the Hubble Space Telescope (HST).\n\n## Key Instruments Installed\n\nThe mission restored the spaceborne observatory's vision (marred by spherical aberration in its mirror) with the installation of a new main camera and a corrective optics package (COSTAR). The astronauts also replaced the original Wide Field Planetary Camera (WFPC) with the more advanced WFPC2 to improve the telescope's ultraviolet performance. The WFPC2 carried its own corrective optics.\n\n## Corrective Optics Details\n\nThe primary task of one EVA was to replace HST's High Speed Photometer (HSP) with a device called COSTAR. This acronym stands for the Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement system and the unit corrected HST's spherical aberration of the main mirror for all instruments except the WFPC-II camera, which had its own built in corrective optics.\n\n## Landing\n\nLanding of the Shuttle occurred at Kennedy Space Center on Runway 33 at 05:26 UTC on December 13, 1993.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"f1897b06-82dd-4191-a25a-89be8b09fad5","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:43:42.728Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0dd54d86-7818-4373-b8b6-99f686823ce6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

{"parentUuid":"84d840ef-8fcd-4ec8-a51a-f884cd89262d","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"271c1fc1-4de5-49e3-abbc-ab0155b76cec","agentId":"a839aee13819dce56","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01W4HjhfvP1uYfHLVsgfsdKH","type":"tool_result","content":"Web search results for query: \"Hubble servicing missions 2 3A 3B 4 dates shuttle crews instruments installed STS-82 STS-103 STS-109 STS-125\"\n\nLinks: [{\"title\":\"STS-125 - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-125\"},{\"title\":\"Fixing the Hubble Space Telescope: A timeline of NASA's shuttle servicing missions | Space\",\"url\":\"https://www.space.com/space-exploration/hubble-space-telescope/fixing-the-hubble-space-telescope-a-timeline-of-nasas-shuttle-servicing-missions\"},{\"title\":\"Servicing Mission 2 | ESA/Hubble | ESA/Hubble\",\"url\":\"https://esahubble.org/about/history/servicing_mission_2/\"},{\"title\":\"15 Years Ago: STS-125, the Final Hubble Servicing Mission - NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/history/15-years-ago-sts-125-the-final-hubble-servicing-mission/\"},{\"title\":\"STS-125 - NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission/sts-125/\"},{\"title\":\"STS-82 - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-82\"},{\"title\":\"Hubble Astronaut Missions - NASA Science\",\"url\":\"https://science.nasa.gov/mission/hubble/team/astronauts/astronaut-missions/\"},{\"title\":\"Astronaut Missions to Hubble - NASA Science\",\"url\":\"https://science.nasa.gov/mission/hubble/observatory/missions-to-hubble/\"},{\"title\":\"How the Space Shuttle completed a final, daring Hubble repair 15 years ago - NASASpaceFlight.com\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasaspaceflight.com/2024/05/sts-125-400-15-years-later/\"},{\"title\":\"Servicing Mission 1 | ESA/Hubble | ESA/Hubble\",\"url\":\"https://esahubble.org/about/history/servicing_mission_1/\"}]\n\nI've completed the web search on Hubble servicing missions. Here's a comprehensive summary of the information found:\n\n## Hubble Servicing Missions Overview\n\n### Servicing Mission 2 (STS-82)\nThe second Servicing Mission (STS-82) was launched to the space telescope in February 1997. Launched aboard the Space Shuttle Discovery on February 11, 1997 at 3:55:17 am (EST), the STS-82 mission served as the second Hubble Space telescope servicing mission.\n\n**Crew:** Kenneth D. Bowersox (commander), Steven A. Hawley (mission specialist), Scott J. Horowitz (pilot), Joseph R. Tanner (mission specialist), Gregory J. Harbaugh (mission specialist), Mark C. Lee (payload commander), and Steven L. Smith (mission specialist).\n\n**Instruments Installed:** Discovery launched on STS-82 with two new instruments for Hubble - the Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph (STIS) and Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer (NICMOS) replaced Hubble's GHRS and FOS.\n\n### Servicing Mission 3A (STS-103)\nSTS-103 occurred December 19-27, 1999. In SM3A (December 1999), astronauts replaced all six gyros and installed a more powerful computer, a new transmitter, new insulation, and other equipment.\n\n### Servicing Mission 3B (STS-109)\nSpace shuttle Columbia launched STS-109 on March 1, with the primary goal of delivering and installing the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS). Astronauts on SM3B were tasked with replacing Hubble's solar arrays, which had been worn by years of radiation and debris. Though smaller, Hubble's new solar arrays provided between 20% and 30% more power. They also replaced Hubble's Power Control Unit (PCU) and added a new cooling system to increase the lifespan of NICMOS.\n\n### Servicing Mission 4 (STS-125)\nThe launch of the Space Shuttle Atlantis occurred on May 11, 2009, at 2:01 pm EDT. Landing occurred on May 24 at 11:39 am EDT, with the mission lasting a total of just under 13 days.\n\n**Crew:** Commander Scott D. Altman, Pilot Gregory C. Johnson, and Mission Specialists Michael T. Good, K. Megan McArthur, John M. Grunsfeld, Michael J. Massimino, and Andrew J. Feustel.\n\n**Instruments Installed:** Space Shuttle Atlantis carried two new instruments to the Hubble Space Telescope, the Cosmic Origins Spectrograph and the Wide Field Camera 3. Recent NASA estimates peg the shuttle program's cost through the end of last year at $209 billion (in 2010 dollars), and a recent study establishes a retrospective baseline life cycle cost estimate of $254.521 billion (in 2024 dollars).\n\n## Cost Per Mission\n\nWhen calculating average cost per mission, the figures vary depending on methodology:\n\n- With 135 missions, and the total cost of US$192 billion (in 2010 dollars), this gives approximately $1.5 billion per launch over the life of the Shuttle program\n\n- Recent NASA estimates peg the shuttle program's cost through the end of last year at $209 billion (in 2010 dollars), yielding a per-flight cost of nearly $1.6 billion\n\n- Accounting for the entire Space Shuttle program budget, the per-launch cost was $1.642 billion (in 2012)\n\n## Variable Launch Costs\n\nThe actual cost to conduct a single launch was considerably lower when excluding development and infrastructure costs. As of 2011, NASA's figure for average cost to launch a Space Shuttle was about $450 million per mission.\n\nThese high per-mission costs reflected not just the launch itself, but the extensive ongoing infrastructure, maintenance, personnel, and operations required to keep the Space Shuttle program operational.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"36d27eb3-b0a0-4c4b-9cd1-cf3509f9e34e","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:44:09.694Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7b22b331-c19b-4aaf-9d9e-4a2df548cbf3","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

{"parentUuid":"686836c7-f241-4c8f-9539-7cb16183767c","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"271c1fc1-4de5-49e3-abbc-ab0155b76cec","agentId":"a839aee13819dce56","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01V2gMVKcTEZCK7oshNG5pAq","type":"tool_result","content":"Web search results for query: \"Hubble Space Telescope mirror Perkin-Elmer null corrector error details spherical aberration 2.2 micron conic constant\"\n\nLinks: [{\"title\":\"Hubble's Mirror Flaw - NASA Science\",\"url\":\"https://science.nasa.gov/mission/hubble/observatory/design/optics/hubbles-mirror-flaw/\"},{\"title\":\"What was wrong with Hubble's mirror, and how was it fixed? | BBC Sky at Night Magazine\",\"url\":\"https://www.skyatnightmagazine.com/space-missions/what-was-wrong-with-hubble-mirror-how-was-it-fixed\"},{\"title\":\"Hubble Space Telescope | Sologic\",\"url\":\"https://www.sologic.com/en-us/resources/example-problems/hubble-space-telescope\"},{\"title\":\"Mega-Projects & -Problems; The Hubble in Trouble\",\"url\":\"https://www.npl.washington.edu/av/altvw44.html\"},{\"title\":\"Hubble’s troublesome mirror | IOPSpark\",\"url\":\"https://spark.iop.org/hubbles-troublesome-mirror\"},{\"title\":\"The Hubble Telescope - ODtid Failure Rewrt November 1930\",\"url\":\"https://ntrs.nasa.gov/api/citations/19910003124/downloads/19910003124.pdf\"},{\"title\":\"Hubble Space Telescope - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope\"},{\"title\":\"The Hubble Space Telescope Optical Systems Failure ...\",\"url\":\"https://www.governmentattic.org/59docs/NASAhubbleOpSystFailureRpt1990.pdf\"},{\"title\":\"How NASA fixed Hubble's flawed vision - and reputation - CBS News\",\"url\":\"https://www.cbsnews.com/news/an-ingenius-fix-for-hubbles-famously-flawed-vision/\"},{\"title\":\"How Hubble Space Telescope failed\",\"url\":\"https://wp.optics.arizona.edu/optomech/wp-content/uploads/sites/53/2016/10/521-synopsis-Tianquan-Su.pdf\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here are the key details about the Hubble Space Telescope mirror error:\n\n## The Null Corrector Error\n\nThe lens in the reflective null corrector (RNC) was incorrectly spaced from the mirrors by 1.3 mm, which accounts in detail for the amount and character of the observed image blurring. The reflective null corrector (RNC), consisted of two small mirrors and a lens and was designed and built by the Perkin-Elmer Corporation for the HST Project.\n\n## The Spherical Aberration Details\n\nBecause the outer edge of the mirror was flatter than specified, light landing on that outer edge had different focal points than the other parts of the mirror. This flaw, called spherical aberration, gave Hubble's primary mirror more than one focal point, which made its images blurry.\n\nThe primary mirror's surface deviates by a distance of about half a wavelength of visible light from its required hyperboloid shape toward the shape of a sphere. The specification of the HST mirror requires an accuracy of 1/70 of a wavelength or better, so the mirror is a factor of 35 out of spec.\n\n## The Conic Constant\n\nThe conic constant of the mirror as built was −1.01390±0.0002, instead of the intended −1.00230. This deviation in the conic constant parameter reflects the fundamental shape error introduced by the null corrector setup mistake.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"faacc143-0454-4fd0-8447-35ac20b88d03","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:44:23.181Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"686836c7-f241-4c8f-9539-7cb16183767c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Let me compile the complete structured enrichment data.\n\n---\n\n# NASA Space Shuttle Era Missions — Structured Enrichment Data (v1.101–v1.127)\n\n## 1.101 — Solar Maximum Mission (SMM)\n\n**Launch:** February 14, 1980, 15:57:00 UTC\n**Launch Vehicle:** Delta 3910 (D-151) from Cape Canaveral AFS, LC-17A\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 2,315 kg (5,104 lb)\n**Manufacturer:** Fairchild Industries (Multimission Modular Spacecraft bus)\n**Dimensions:** 4.0 x 2.3 m\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 508 km | Apogee: 512 km\n- Inclination: 28.5 deg | Period: 94.80 min\n- Eccentricity: 0.00029\n\n**Instruments (7):**\n1. Coronagraph/Polarimeter (446.5-658.3 nm)\n2. Ultraviolet Spectrometer (175.0-360.0 nm)\n3. Soft X-ray Polychromator (0.14-2.25 nm)\n4. Hard X-ray Imaging Spectrometer (3.5-30 keV)\n5. Hard X-ray Burst Spectrometer (20-260 keV)\n6. Gamma-ray Spectrometer (0.01-100 MeV)\n7. Active Cavity Radiometer Irradiance Monitor (ACRIM)\n\n**Primary Objectives:** Coordinated observations of solar activity, particularly solar flares, during solar maximum.\n\n**Key Results:**\n- ACRIM revealed the Sun is brighter during sunspot cycle maximum (faculae offset darkening)\n- Discovered 10 Kreutz sungrazer comets (nuclei <= 16 m radius)\n- Attitude control failed November 1989; solar arrays degraded\n\n**Repair Mission (STS-41-C, April 1984):** Astronauts James van Hoften and George Nelson replaced the entire attitude control system module and coronagraph electronics. First orbiting unmanned satellite repaired in space. Extended lifespan ~5 years.\n\n**Reentry:** December 2, 1989, over Indian Ocean\n**Mission Duration:** 9 years, 9 months, 18 days\n\n---\n\n## 1.102 — STS-1 Columbia (First Shuttle Flight)\n\n**Launch:** April 12, 1981, 12:00:04 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** April 14, 1981, 18:20:57 UTC at Edwards AFB, Runway 23\n**Duration:** 2 days, 6 hours, 20 minutes, 53 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Space Shuttle Columbia (OV-102)\n\n**Crew (2):**\n- Commander: John W. Young (5th spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Robert L. Crippen (1st spaceflight)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 99,453 kg | Landing 88,662 kg | Payload (DFI) 4,909 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 246 km | Apogee: 274 km\n- Inclination: 40.30 deg | Period: 89.88 min\n- Orbits: 36 | Distance: 1,729,348 km\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First orbital Space Shuttle flight\n- First American crewed spaceflight since Apollo-Soyuz (1975)\n- First crewed launch using solid-fuel rocket boosters\n- Launched exactly 20 years after Vostok 1 (coincidental — scrubbed April 10)\n\n**Key Issues:** ~70 anomalies including 16 undensified tiles lost from OMS/RCS pods, hot gas ducting into landing gear well from improperly installed tiles.\n\n**All 113 flight test objectives accomplished.**\n\n**Cost:** ~$1.5 billion per Shuttle mission (program average in 2010 dollars); ~$450 million marginal per-mission cost.\n\n---\n\n## 1.103 — STS-2 Columbia\n\n**Launch:** November 12, 1981, 15:10:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** November 14, 1981, 21:23:12 UTC at Edwards AFB, Runway 23\n**Duration:** 2 days, 6 hours, 13 minutes, 12 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Columbia (OV-102) — first reuse of a crewed orbital spacecraft\n\n**Crew (2):**\n- Commander: Joe H. Over 90% of mission objectives achieved despite shortened duration.\n\n**Notable First:** First reuse of a crewed orbital vehicle.\n\n---\n\n## 1.104 — STS-3 Columbia\n\n**Launch:** March 22, 1982, 16:00:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** March 30, 1982, 16:04:46 UTC at White Sands Space Harbor, Northrop Strip Runway 17\n**Duration:** 8 days, 0 hours, 4 minutes, 46 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Columbia (OV-102)\n\n**Crew (2):**\n- Commander: Jack R. Anik C-3 (Telesat Canada) — Hughes HS-376 with PAM-D kick motor\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First operational Space Shuttle mission\n- First commercial satellite deployment from Shuttle\n- Largest crew SLC-2W\n**Launch Vehicle:** Delta 3910\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 1,083 kg (2,388 lb)\n**Dimensions:** 3.60 x 3.24 x 2.05 m\n**Manufacturers:** Ball Aerospace, Fokker Space, Hollandse Signaal\n\n**International Partners:** NASA (USA), NIVR (Netherlands), SERC (United Kingdom)\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Sun-synchronous geocentric orbit\n- Altitude: ~880-906 km\n- Inclination: 98.95 deg | Period: 102.8 min\n\n**Primary Instrument:** 57 cm Ritchey-Chretien telescope, focal length 545 cm (f/9.56), collecting area 2,019 cm2\n**Detectors:** Survey Array (SA), Low Resolution Spectrometer (LRS), Chopped Photometric Channel (CPC)\n**Wavelength Bands:** 12, 25, 60, 100 micrometers\n**Coolant:** 73 kg superfluid helium at 2 K (-271 C)\n**Resolution:** 30 arcseconds to 2 arcminutes\n**Sky Coverage:** 96% mapped four times\n\n**Mission Timeline:**\n- Entered service: February 9, 1983\n- Helium depletion: November 21, 1983\n- Mission duration: ~10 months\n\n**Major Discoveries:**\n- 250,000+ infrared sources cataloged (~350,000 total), including ~75,000 starburst galaxies\n- Dust disk around Vega (first protoplanetary disk detected around a main-sequence star)\n- First infrared images of Milky Way's core\n- \"Intergalactic cirrus\" identified\n- 4 asteroids discovered, including 3200 Phaethon (Geminid meteor shower parent body)\n- 6 comets discovered, including C/1983 H1 (IRAS-Araki-Alcock)\n- Dust trail associated with 10P/Tempel\n\n**Notable First:** First space telescope to perform an all-sky survey at infrared wavelengths.\n\n---\n\n## 1.108 — hours, 23 minutes, 42 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Space Shuttle Challenger (OV-099) — maiden flight\n\n**Crew (4):**\n- Commander: Paul J. Weitz (2nd spaceflight; Skylab-2 veteran)\n- Pilot: Karol J. Bobko (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: F. Story Musgrave (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2: Donald H. Peterson (only spaceflight)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 116,457 kg | Landing 86,330 kg | Payload 21,305 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 288 km | Apogee: 295 km\n- Inclination: 28.50 deg | Period: 90.40 min\n- Orbits: 81 | Distance: 3,370,437 km\n\n**Primary Payload: TDRS-1**\n- 2,300 kg Tracking and Data Relay Satellite deployed successfully\n- IUS booster malfunctioned, placing satellite in wrong orbit\n- Saved via gradual thruster repositioning to geosynchronous orbit over months ($100 million satellite saved)\n\n**EVA:** April 7-8, 1983 (21:03-01:20 UTC) — 4 hours, 17 minutes\n- Musgrave and Peterson performed first Shuttle program EVA\n- First American spacewalk since Skylab 4 (nearly a decade)\n- Used new Extravehicular Mobility Unit spacesuits designed for Shuttle\n\n**Other Firsts:**\n- Maiden flight of Challenger\n- First use of lightweight external tank and SRB casings\n- First head-up display on Shuttle\n\n---\n\n## 1.109 — STS-7 (Sally Ride — First American Woman in Space)\n\n**Launch:** June 18, 1983, 11:33:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** June 24, 1983, 13:56:59 UTC at Edwards AFB, Runway 15\n**Duration:** 6 days, 2 hours, 23 minutes, 59 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Challenger (OV-099)\n\n**Crew (5):**\n- Commander: Robert Crippen (2nd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Frederick Hauck (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: John M. Fabian (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2/Flight Engineer: Sally Ride (1st spaceflight) — **first American woman in space**\n- Mission Specialist 3: Norman Thagard (1st spaceflight)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 113,025 kg | Landing 92,550 kg | Payload 16,839 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 299 km | Apogee: 307 km\n- Inclination: 28.30 deg | Period: 90.60 min\n- Orbits: 97 | Distance: 3,570,000 km\n\n**Satellite Deployments:**\n1. Anik C2 (Telesat Canada) — Hughes HS-376\n2. Palapa B1 (Indonesia) — Hughes HS-376\n\n**SPAS-01:** First Shuttle Pallet Satellite (West German MBB), deployed and retrieved by Ride using Canadarm.\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First American woman in space (Valentina Tereshkova was first woman overall, 1963)\n- Largest crew to date (five astronauts)\n- First Group 8 (1978) astronaut class members on Shuttle\n- First astronaut (Crippen) to make second Shuttle flight\n- First external tank foam shedding incident observed\n\n---\n\n## 1.110 — STS-8 (Guy Bluford — First African American in Space)\n\n**Launch:** August 30, 1983, 06:32:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A (night launch)\n**Landing:** September 5, 1983, 07:40:43 UTC at Edwards AFB (night landing)\n**Duration:** 6 days, 1 hour, 8 minutes, 43 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Challenger (OV-099)\n\n**Crew (5):**\n- Commander: Richard H. Truly (2nd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Daniel Brandenstein (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Guion \"Guy\" Bluford (1st spaceflight) — **first African American in space**\n- Mission Specialist 2/Flight Engineer: Dale Gardner (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: William E. Thornton (1st spaceflight)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 110,108 kg | Landing 92,508 kg | Payload 12,011 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 348 km | Apogee: 356 km\n- Inclination: 28.51 deg | Period: 90.60 min\n- Orbits: 98\n\n**Primary Payload: INSAT-1B**\n- Indian communications/weather satellite deployed via PAM-D\n- Total mass with cradle: 4,479 kg\n- Provided 7 years of service; returned 36,000 images\n\n**Secondary Payload:** 3,855 kg aluminum Payload Flight Test Article for Canadarm testing (achieved 5 cm / 1 degree precision)\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First night launch and first night landing of Space Shuttle\n- First African American in space\n\n**Critical Finding:** Post-flight SRB inspection revealed nozzle resin eroded to 0.5 cm in places — approximately 14 seconds from catastrophic rupture. Lowest tile damage record at that date.\n\n---\n\n## 1.111 — STS-41-B (First Untethered Spacewalk)\n\n**Launch:** February 3, 1984, 13:00:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** February 11, 1984, 12:15:55 UTC at KSC SLF Runway 15\n**Duration:** 7 days, 23 hours, 15 minutes, 55 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Challenger (OV-099)\n\n**Crew (5):**\n- Commander: Vance D. Brand (3rd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Robert L. Gibson (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Bruce McCandless II (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2/Flight Engineer: Robert L. Stewart (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: Ronald E. McNair (1st spaceflight; later lost in STS-51-L)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 113,603 kg | Landing 91,280 kg | Payload 12,815 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 307 km | Apogee: 317 km\n- Inclination: 28.50 deg | Period: 90.80 min\n- Orbits: 128 | Distance: 5,329,150 km\n\n**Satellite Deployments (both malfunctioned):**\n1. Westar 6 (Western Union) — PAM-D failed, stranded in low orbit\n2. Palapa B2 (Indonesia) — PAM-D failed, stranded in low orbit\n(Both later retrieved by STS-51-A)\n\n**EVAs:**\n- EVA 1 (Feb 7): 5 hours, 55 min — McCandless and Stewart. **First untethered spacewalk** using Manned Maneuvering Unit (MMU). McCandless reached 98 m (322 ft) from orbiter.\n- EVA 2 (Feb 9): 6 hours, 17 min — McCandless and Stewart. Practiced Solar Max capture procedures for upcoming STS-41-C.\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First untethered EVA in history (MMU)\n- First spacecraft landing at its own launch site (KSC)\n- First high school student experiment in space (Brighton High School, Utah)\n\n---\n\n## 1.112 — STS-41-C (First Satellite Repair)\n\n**Launch:** April 6, 1984, 13:58:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** April 13, 1984, 13:38:07 UTC at Edwards AFB, Runway 17\n**Duration:** 6 days, 23 hours, 40 minutes, 7 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Challenger (OV-099)\n\n**Crew (5):**\n- Commander: Robert Crippen (3rd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Dick Scobee (1st spaceflight; later lost as STS-51-L commander)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Terry Hart (only spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2: James van Hoften (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: George Nelson (1st spaceflight)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 115,328 kg | Landing 89,346 kg | Payload 15,345 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 222 km | Apogee: 428 km\n- Inclination: 28.50 deg | Period: 91.40 min\n- Orbits: 108\n\n**LDEF Deployment:** Long Duration Exposure Facility (9,700 kg, 4.3 m dia x 9.1 m) carrying 57 experiments from 200 researchers across 8 countries, deployed by Canadarm.\n\n**Solar Maximum Mission Repair:**\n- EVA 1 (April 8): 2 hours, 59 min — Nelson attempted capture using MMU + Trunnion Pin Acquisition Device (TPAD). Three clamping attempts failed; satellite set tumbling.\n- EVA 2 (April 11): 7 hours, 7 min — Successful repair. Replaced attitude control system and coronagraph electronics.\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First satellite repair in orbit\n- First direct ascent trajectory for a Shuttle mission (single OMS burn)\n\n---\n\n## 1.113 — STS-41-D (First Discovery Flight)\n\n**Launch:** August 30, 1984, 12:41:50 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** September 5, 1984, 13:37:55 UTC at Edwards AFB, Runway 17\n**Duration:** 6 days, 0 hours, 56 minutes, 4 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Space Shuttle Discovery (OV-103) — maiden flight\n\n**Crew (6):**\n- Commander: Henry W. Hartsfield Jr. (2nd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Michael L. Coats (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Richard M. Mullane (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2: Steven A. Hawley (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: Judith A. Resnik (1st spaceflight; later lost in STS-51-L)\n- Payload Specialist 1: Charles D. Walker — **first commercial payload specialist**\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 119,511 kg | Landing 91,418 kg | Payload 18,681 kg (record at that time)\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 346 km | Apogee: 354 km\n- Inclination: 28.50 deg | Period: 90.60 min\n- Orbits: 97 | Distance: 4,010,000 km\n\n**Satellite Deployments (record 3):**\n1. SBS-4 (Satellite Business Systems)\n2. Telstar 302 (AT&T)\n3. Syncom IV-2/Leasat-2 (U.S. Navy) — first satellite designed for Shuttle launch\n\n**OAST-1 Solar Array:** 4 m wide x 31 m tall experimental extendable solar array. Demonstrated feasibility for future ISS solar arrays.\n\n**Launch Abort (June 26, 1984):** Pad abort at T-4 seconds followed by pad fire ~10 minutes later. Stuck valve prevented LOX flow to engine 3. Hydrogen fire from free fuel around engine nozzles. Crew evacuated safely.\n\n**Critical Finding:** First STS mission where SRB O-ring \"blow-by\" was discovered — soot found beyond primary O-ring. Later identified as precursor to Challenger disaster.\n\n---\n\n## 1.114 — STS-51-A (Satellite Retrieval)\n\n**Launch:** November 8, 1984, 12:15:00 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** November 16, 1984, 11:59:56 UTC at KSC SLF Runway 15\n**Duration:** 7 days, 23 hours, 44 minutes, 56 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Discovery (OV-103)\n\n**Crew (5):**\n- Commander: Frederick H. Hauck (2nd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: David M. Walker (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Joseph P. Allen (2nd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2: Anna Lee Fisher (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: Dale A. Gardner (2nd spaceflight)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 119,442 kg | Landing 94,123 kg | Payload 17,375 kg\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 332 km | Apogee: 354 km\n- Inclination: 28.45 deg | Period: 90.40 min\n- Orbits: 127\n\n**Satellite Deployments:**\n1. Anik D2 (Canada)\n2. Syncom IV-1/Leasat 1 (U.S. Navy)\n\n**Satellite Retrievals (both stranded from STS-41-B):**\n1. Palapa B2 — Recovered mission day 5. Used \"Stinger\" (Apogee Capture Device) inserted into apogee motor nozzle. Manual rescue took ~2 hours.\n2. Westar 6 — Recovered day 6 using same technique. Easier retrieval.\n\n**EVAs:**\n- EVA 1 (Nov 12): 6 hours, 0 min — Allen and Gardner\n- EVA 2 (Nov 14): 5 hours, 42 min — Allen and Gardner\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First mission to both deploy and retrieve satellites\n- Last untethered spacewalks until 1994\n- Last use of Manned Maneuvering Unit (MMU)\n\n**\"The Ace Repo Company\"** — crew nickname for the mission.\n\n---\n\n## 1.115 — STS-51-L Challenger Disaster\n\n**Launch:** January 28, 1986, 16:38:00 UTC from KSC LC-39B\n**Launch Temperature:** 27 deg F (-3 deg C) air; right SRB measured at 8 deg F (-13 deg C) overnight\n**Mission Duration:** 73 seconds (destroyed in flight)\n**Orbiter:** Challenger (OV-099)\n\n**Crew (7, all lost):**\n- Commander: Francis R. \"Dick\" Scobee (2nd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Michael J. Smith (1st spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Ellison S. Onizuka (2nd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2: Judith A. Resnik (2nd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: Ronald E. McNair (2nd spaceflight)\n- Payload Specialist 1: S. Christa McAuliffe (Teacher in Space Project)\n- Payload Specialist 2: Gregory B. Jarvis (Hughes Space Communications)\n\n**Mass:** Liftoff 1,217,990 kg | Planned landing 90,584 kg | Payload 21,937 kg\n\n**Planned Payloads:**\n- TDRS-B (Tracking and Data Relay Satellite-B) with IUS\n- SPARTAN-203 (Halley's Comet observation)\n- CHAMP (Comet Halley Active Monitoring Program)\n- Teacher in Space Project lessons\n\n**O-Ring Failure Technical Details:**\n- Root cause: failure of primary and secondary O-ring seals on right SRB field joint\n- O-rings: 20-foot diameter Viton rubber, 0.280-inch cross-section\n- Joint design: tang-and-clevis configuration with putty gap fill\n- 1977 testing revealed 0.052 inches of joint rotation under pressure, weakening O-ring seals\n- Cold temperature eliminated O-ring elasticity; unable to seal joint gap\n- Hot gas impinged on aft strut; strut failed at T+73 seconds\n- Forward SRB cylinder struck external tank inter-tank area\n- Structural failure of external tank caused rapid propellant combustion\n- Orbiter destroyed by overwhelming aerodynamic forces during breakup\n\n**Temperature Data:**\n- Launch temperature: 27 deg F (-3 deg C) — well below 39 deg F SRB qualification minimum\n- Right SRB measured overnight at 8 deg F (-13 deg C)\n- Previous coldest launch: STS-51-C (January 1985) at 53 deg F (12 deg C) O-ring temp — already showed erosion\n- Morton Thiokol engineers recommended \"not to launch below 53 deg F\"\n\n**Morton Thiokol Engineer Warnings:**\n- Roger Boisjoly, Bob Ebeling, and Allan McDonald strongly opposed launch\n- McDonald refused to sign launch recommendation as on-site representative\n- Thiokol management reversed engineer position, calling evidence \"inconclusive\"\n- NASA SRB manager Lawrence Mulloy: \"Do you want me to wait until April?\"\n\n**Feynman Ice Water Demonstration:**\n- February 11, 1986, during televised Rogers Commission hearing\n- Physicist Richard Feynman immersed rubber O-ring sample in glass of ice water\n- Demonstrated loss of elasticity at cold temperatures\n- Became iconic engineering failure demonstration\n- Feynman threatened to remove his name from report unless personal observations included (became Appendix F)\n\n**Rogers Commission Key Findings (released June 9, 1986):**\n1. Redesign SRB field joints to prevent gas leakage\n2. Restructured management to prevent unsafe schedule pressure\n3. Independent safety office reporting to NASA administrator\n4. Improved crew escape capabilities\n5. Enhanced training and quality control\n6. Astronaut involvement in safety decisions\n7. Risk management reviews for all critical systems\n8. Increased reliability assessment\n9. Improved communication protocols\n- Concluded: \"safety culture and management structure at NASA were insufficient\"\n\n**Crew Cabin Forensics:**\n- Crew cabin separated intact from orbiter\n- Traveled ballistic arc reaching 65,000 feet before falling at terminal velocity\n- 3 of 4 recovered Personal Egress Air Packs had been manually activated (indicating consciousness)\n- Impact velocity: 207 mph, ~200 g deceleration (unsurvivable)\n- Remains recovered March 7-8, 1986 from ocean floor\n\n**Redesigned SRB Joint (RSRM):**\n- Capture feature on tang interior (reduced rotation to 15% of disaster level)\n- Additional third O-ring\n- Heaters to maintain consistent O-ring temperature\n- Tested with intentional flaws April/August 1988\n\n**Program Impact:** 32-month flight suspension. Return to flight: STS-26, September 1988.\n\n---\n\n## 1.116 — Magellan (Venus Radar Mapper)\n\n**Launch:** May 4, 1989, 18:46:59 UTC from KSC LC-39B aboard STS-30 (Atlantis)\n**Deployment:** May 5, 1989, 01:06:00 UTC with IUS booster\n**Venus Arrival:** August 10, 1990\n**Mission End:** October 13, 1994, 10:05:00 UTC (controlled deorbit into Venus atmosphere)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** Dry 1,035 kg | Total launch 3,445 kg\n**Dimensions:** 6.4 m tall, 4.6 m diameter\n**Power:** ~1,030 watts (solar)\n**Manufacturer:** Martin Marietta (bus); Hughes Aircraft (radar)\n\n**Venus Orbital Parameters:**\n- Periapsis: 295 km (later 180 km after aerobraking)\n- Apoapsis: 7,762 km (later 540 km)\n- Period: 3.26 hours (elliptical) | Inclination: 85.5 deg\n\n**Instrument: Radar System (RDRS)**\n- 3.7 m parabolic high-gain antenna\n- Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR): 12.6 cm wavelength, 120-300 m resolution\n- Altimetry (ALT): 50 km spatial, 100 m vertical resolution\n- Radiometry (RAD): surface temperature\n- Data rate: 750 kbit/s\n\n**Mapping Results:**\n| Cycle | Period | Coverage |\n|-------|--------|----------|\n| 1 | Sept 1990-May 1991 | 83.7% |\n| 2 | May 1991-Jan 1992 | +54.5% |\n| 3 | Jan-Sept 1992 | Stereo; total 98% |\n| 4-6 | Sept 1992-Oct 1994 | Gravity + high-res |\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- 85%+ of Venus surface covered in volcanic flows\n- Vast lava plains, shield volcanoes, pancake-shaped domes\n- Lava channels over 6,000 km long\n- Few impact craters — geologically young surface (<800 Myr)\n- Global rift zones and coronae; no plate tectonics\n- Limited wind erosion despite dense atmosphere\n\n**Aerobraking:** May 24-Aug 2, 1993. First spacecraft to use aerobraking for orbit circularization.\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First interplanetary mission launched from Space Shuttle\n- First to use IUS for interplanetary flight\n- First spacecraft to test aerobraking\n\n---\n\n## 1.117 — Galileo (Jupiter Orbiter & Probe)\n\n**Launch:** October 18, 1989, 16:53:40 UTC from KSC LC-39B aboard STS-34 (Atlantis)\n**Jupiter Arrival:** December 7, 1995\n**Mission End:** September 21, 2003 (deliberate crash into Jupiter to protect Europa)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** Orbiter 2,220 kg (1,880 kg dry) | Probe 340 kg | Total 2,560 kg\n**Manufacturer:** JPL (orbiter); Hughes Aircraft (probe)\n\n**Orbiter Instruments (10):**\n- Solid-State Imager (SSI) — 800x800 CCD camera\n- Near-Infrared Mapping Spectrometer (NIMS)\n- Ultraviolet/EUV Spectrometer\n- Photopolarimeter-Radiometer (PPR)\n- Dust-Detector Subsystem (DDS)\n- Energetic-Particles Detector (EPD)\n- Heavy-Ion Counter (HIC)\n- Magnetometer (MAG) — 11 m boom\n- Plasma Subsystem (PLS)\n- Plasma-Wave Subsystem (PWS)\n\n**Atmospheric Probe Instruments (7):** Atmospheric structure instrument, neutral mass spectrometer, helium abundance detector, nephelometer, net-flux radiometer, lightning/energetic particles detector, radio equipment.\n\n**Probe Entry (Dec 7, 1995):** 48 km/s entry speed, peak temperature ~16,000 deg C.\n\n**Trajectory:** Venus flyby (Feb 1990) → Earth flyby (Dec 1990, Dec 1992) → Asteroid 951 Gaspra (Oct 1991) → Asteroid 243 Ida (Aug 1993, discovered moon Dactyl) → Jupiter (Dec 1995)\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- Subsurface liquid saltwater oceans on Europa, Ganymede, Callisto\n- Extensive volcanic activity on Io (100+ active volcanoes)\n- Ganymede has its own magnetic field\n- Jupiter's faint ring system composed of small particles\n- Observed Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9 impact on Jupiter (1994)\n- First asteroid flyby (Gaspra); first asteroid moon discovery (Dactyl)\n\n**High-Gain Antenna Failure:** Failed to deploy fully; entire mission conducted via low-gain antenna with severely limited data rates.\n\n**Total Cost:** $1.39 billion (+ $110 million international contributions)\n**Mission Duration:** 13 years, 11 months, 3 days (planned: ~8 years)\n\n---\n\n## 1.118 — COBE (Cosmic Background Explorer)\n\n**Launch:** November 18, 1989, 14:34:00 UTC from Vandenberg SLC-2W\n**Launch Vehicle:** Delta 5920-8 (Delta 189)\n**Deactivated:** December 23, 1993\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** Launch 2,206 kg | Dry 1,408 kg\n**Dimensions:** 5.49 x 2.44 m\n**Power:** 750 watts\n**Manufacturer:** Goddard Space Flight Center\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Sun-synchronous orbit\n- Altitude: 900 km circular\n- Inclination: 99.00 deg | Period: 103 min\n\n**Three Instruments:**\n\n1. **FIRAS** (Far Infrared Absolute Spectrophotometer) — PI: John C. Mather\n   - Cryogenically cooled Michelson interferometer / Fourier transform spectrometer\n   - Range: 0.1-10 mm wavelengths\n   - Mass: 60 kg | Power: 84 W | Data rate: 1,200 bit/s\n\n2. **DMR** (Differential Microwave Radiometer) — PI: George F. Smoot III\n   - Three radiometers at 31.4, 53, and 90 GHz\n   - Sensitivity: ~3x10^-5 K\n   - Mass: 120 kg | Power: 114 W | Data rate: 500 bit/s\n\n3. **DIRBE** (Diffuse Infrared Background Experiment) — PI: Michael G. Hauser\n   - Cryogenically cooled multiband radiometer (1-300 micrometers)\n   - 10 wavelength bands, 1 deg field of view\n   - Mass: 34 kg | Power: 100 W | Data rate: 1,700 bit/s\n\n**Key Results:**\n\n**CMB Blackbody Spectrum:** Perfect fit between predicted big bang black body curve and observed microwave background at 2.725 K temperature — contradicted competing 1987 observations.\n\n**CMB Anisotropy:** Detected temperature variations of 1 part in 100,000 compared to the 2.73 K average — \"primordial seeds\" of structure formation.\n\n**Announcement:** April 23, 1992, APS April Meeting, Washington DC. Front-page New York Times next day.\n\n**2006 Nobel Prize in Physics:** Awarded to John C. Mather (NASA/GSFC) and George F. Smoot III (UC Berkeley) \"for their discovery of the blackbody form and anisotropy of the cosmic microwave background radiation.\"\n\nNobel committee: \"The COBE project can also be regarded as the starting point for cosmology as a precision science.\"\n\n**Mission Duration:** 4 years, 1 month, 4 days (planned: 6 months)\n**Cost:** Under $30 million (NASA requirement, excluding launcher and data analysis)\n\n---\n\n## 1.119 — Hubble Space Telescope\n\n**Launch:** April 24, 1990, 12:33:51 UTC from KSC LC-39B aboard STS-31 (Discovery)\n**Deployment:** April 25, 1990\n**Current Status:** Still operational (expected through 2030-2040)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 11,110 kg (24,490 lb)\n**Dimensions:** 13.2 m x 4.2 m\n**Power:** 2,800 watts\n**Total Cost:** ~$4.7 billion launch (2010 dollars); ~$11.3 billion cumulative (2015 dollars) — most expensive science mission in NASA history\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Altitude: ~540 km\n- Inclination: 28.47 deg | Period: 95.42 min\n\n**Optical Specifications:**\n- Type: Ritchey-Chretien reflector\n- Primary mirror diameter: 2.4 m (7 ft 10 in)\n- Focal length: 57.6 m | f-ratio: f/24\n- Collecting area: 4.0 m2\n\n**Mirror Spherical Aberration:**\n- Error: outer perimeter too flat by 2,200 nanometers (~1/50 human hair thickness)\n- Cause: reflective null corrector at Perkin-Elmer had one lens out of position by 1.3 mm\n- Conic constant as built: -1.01390 +/- 0.0002 (intended: -1.00230)\n- Mirror out of specification by factor of 35 (specification required 1/70 wavelength accuracy)\n- Announced June 27, 1990 (2 months after launch)\n\n**COSTAR Fix:** Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement — 5 pairs of corrective mirrors for FOC, FOS, and GHRS instruments. Installed SM1 (1993), removed SM4 (2009) to make room for COS.\n\n**Five Servicing Missions:**\n\n| Mission | Date | Shuttle | Commander | Key Actions |\n|---------|------|---------|-----------|-------------|\n| SM1 (STS-61) | Dec 2-13, 1993 | Endeavour | Richard Covey | Installed COSTAR + WFPC2; replaced solar arrays, 4 gyros; 5 EVAs (35h 28m record) |\n| SM2 (STS-82) | Feb 11-21, 1997 | Discovery | Kenneth Bowersox | Installed STIS + NICMOS; replaced FOS + GHRS |\n| SM3A (STS-103) | Dec 19-27, 1999 | Discovery | Curtis Brown | Replaced all 6 gyros; new computer, transmitter, insulation (emergency after 4th gyro failed) |\n| SM3B (STS-109) | Mar 1-12, 2002 | Columbia | Scott Altman | Installed ACS (replaced FOC); new solar arrays (+30% power); NICMOS cooler |\n| SM4 (STS-125) | May 11-24, 2009 | Atlantis | Scott Altman | Installed WFC3 + COS; removed COSTAR; repaired STIS + ACS; new batteries, gyros, science computer. Cost ~$500M (not including shuttle) |\n\n**Key Scientific Discoveries:**\n- Hubble Deep Field (1995): ~3,000 galaxies in a tiny patch, 4 billion times fainter than human eye\n- Refined Hubble Constant to 1% precision → universe age ~13.8 billion years (narrowed from 9.7-19.5 billion range)\n- Contributed to 1998 discovery of accelerating universe expansion (dark energy) via Type Ia supernovae\n- Detected supermassive black holes at centers of galaxies\n- Characterized atmospheres of exoplanets\n- Observed Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9 impact on Jupiter\n- Pillars of Creation image (Eagle Nebula, 1995) — iconic\n\n**Active Instruments:** ACS, COS, STIS, WFC3 (NICMOS in hibernation)\n\n---\n\n## 1.120 — Ulysses (Polar Solar Orbit)\n\n**Launch:** October 6, 1990, 11:47:16 UTC from KSC LC-39B aboard STS-41 (Discovery)\n**Jupiter Gravity Assist:** February 8, 1992 (closest approach 440,439 km)\n**Mission End:** June 30, 2009 (loss of contact)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 371 kg (818 lb)\n**Dimensions:** 3.2 x 3.3 x 2.1 m\n**Power:** 285 watts (GPHS-RTG radioisotope generator)\n**Manufacturer:** Dornier Systems (now Astrium), Germany\n**International Partners:** ESA (mission lead), NASA (launch + RTG)\n**Design Life:** 5 years (operated 18.7 years)\n\n**Upper Stage:** IUS + PAM-S — fastest artificially-accelerated spacecraft upon leaving Earth (until New Horizons)\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Heliocentric orbit\n- Perihelion: ~1.35 AU | Aphelion: ~5.40 AU\n- Inclination: 79.11 deg (to ecliptic)\n- Period: ~6.2 years\n\n**Instruments (12):** MAG, SWOOPS, SWICS, URAP, EPAC/GAS, HI-SCALE, COSPIN, GRB, DUST, radio/plasma antennas (72 m dipole), experiment boom\n\n**Solar Polar Passages:**\n- First: 1994-1995 (solar minimum)\n- Second: 2000-2001 (solar maximum)\n- Third: 2007-2008 (minimum, reversed polarity)\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- Solar wind speed at high latitudes leveled off at 750 km/s (did not increase continuously toward poles)\n- Sun's magnetic field interacts with solar system more complexly than assumed\n- Interstellar dust 30x more abundant than previously expected\n- Solar polar magnetic field significantly weaker than measured\n- Solar wind progressively weakened during mission\n\n**Comet Tail Encounters (3):**\n- Hyakutake (May 1996) — tail at least 3.8 AU long\n- McNaught-Hartley (2004)\n- McNaught (2007)\n\n---\n\n## 1.121 — CGRO (Compton Gamma Ray Observatory)\n\n**Launch:** April 5, 1991, 14:22:45 UTC from KSC LC-39B aboard STS-37 (Atlantis)\n**Deorbit:** June 4, 2000, 23:29:55 UTC (controlled reentry into Pacific Ocean)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 16,329 kg (35,999 lb) — heaviest astrophysical payload flown at that time\n**Cost:** $617 million\n**Manufacturer:** TRW Inc. (now Northrop Grumman)\n**Program:** NASA Great Observatories series (2nd launched after Hubble)\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 362 km | Apogee: 457 km\n- Inclination: 28.46 deg | Period: 91.59 min\n\n**Four Instruments (covering 20 keV - 30 GeV, six decades of energy):**\n\n1. **BATSE** (Burst and Transient Source Experiment): 20 keV to >8 MeV. Eight NaI(Tl) detector modules at satellite corners. Detected ~1 gamma-ray burst/day.\n\n2. **OSSE** (Oriented Scintillation Spectrometer Experiment): 0.05-10 MeV. Four individually pointable modules with NaI(Tl)/CsI(Na) phoswich design.\n\n3. **COMPTEL** (Imaging Compton Telescope): 0.75-30 MeV. One steradian FOV. Angular resolution ~1 degree.\n\n4. **EGRET** (Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope): 20 MeV-30 GeV. Electron-positron pair production detection.\n\n**EVA Note:** Astronaut Jay Apt performed EVA to free partially deployed antenna during STS-37 deployment.\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- First all-sky survey above 100 MeV (EGRET) — 271 sources, 170 unidentified\n- Demonstrated gamma-ray bursts are isotropic (from distant galaxies, not Milky Way)\n- Two GRB classifications: short (<2s) and long (>2s)\n- GRB 990123: first optical afterglow during prompt emission (redshift 1.6, distance 3.2 Gpc)\n- Detected terrestrial gamma-ray sources from thunderclouds (1994)\n- Completed aluminum-26 isotope sky map\n\n**Mission Duration:** 9 years, 2 months\n**Deorbit Debris:** Six 1,800-lb aluminum I-beams + titanium parts (~5,000 bolts) fell into Pacific\n**First NASA intentional controlled satellite deorbit.**\n\n---\n\n## 1.122 — UARS (Upper Atmosphere Research Satellite)\n\n**Launch:** September 12, 1991, 23:11:04 UTC from KSC LC-39A aboard STS-48 (Discovery)\n**Deployment:** September 15, 1991\n**Decommissioned:** December 14-15, 2005\n**Reentry:** September 24, 2011, ~04:00 UTC (Pacific Ocean near American Samoa)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** Dry 5,900 kg | Launch 6,540 kg\n**Power:** 1,600 watts\n**Manufacturer:** Martin Marietta (MultiMission Modular Spacecraft bus)\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Altitude: 600 km | Inclination: 57 deg | Period: 95.9 min\n\n**Ten Instruments:**\n1. CLAES — Cryogenic Limb Array Etalon Spectrometer (nitrogen, chlorine, ozone, water vapor, methane)\n2. ISAMS — Improved Stratospheric and Mesospheric Sounder (temp, CO2, N2O, HNO3, CO)\n3. MLS — Microwave Limb Sounder (63, 183, 205 GHz; first global ClO dataset)\n4. HALOE — Halogen Occultation Experiment (8 IR wavelengths; HF, nitrogen family)\n5. HRDI — High Resolution Doppler Imager (oxygen emission/absorption)\n6. WINDII — Wind Imaging Interferometer (wind, temperature, airglow)\n7. SUSIM — Solar UV Spectral Irradiance Monitor\n8. SOLSTICE — Solar Stellar Irradiance Comparison Experiment\n9. ACRIM II — Active Cavity Radiometer Irradiance Monitor\n10. PEM — Particle Environment Monitor\n\n**Key Results:**\n- First global maps of ozone-depleting chlorinated compounds\n- CLAES: 19-month global database of ozone-layer gas distributions\n- MLS: first \"global\" dataset on chlorine monoxide (key ozone destruction intermediate)\n- Confirmed TSI variation occurs on virtually every timescale\n\n**Mission Duration:** 14 years, 3 months (planned: 3 years); 6 of 10 instruments still operational at shutdown.\n\n---\n\n## 1.123 — Mars Observer (Lost)\n\n**Launch:** September 25, 1992, 17:05:01 UTC from Cape Canaveral SLC-40\n**Launch Vehicle:** Commercial Titan III CT-4 with Transfer Orbit Stage\n**Communication Loss:** August 21, 1993, 01:00 UTC (3 days before Mars orbital insertion)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 1,018 kg (bus) + 1,346 kg propellant\n**Bus Dimensions:** 1.1 x 2.2 x 1.6 m\n**Power:** 1,147 watts average\n**Manufacturer:** General Electric Astro Space (RCA AS-4000 bus) / Martin Marietta\n**Total Program Cost:** $813 million\n\n**Seven Instruments:**\n1. Mars Observer Camera (MOC) — narrow/wide-angle telescopes\n2. Mars Observer Laser Altimeter (MOLA) — topography\n3. Thermal Emission Spectrometer (TES) — mineral composition\n4. Pressure Modulator Infrared Radiometer (PMIRR) — atmospheric profiling\n5. Gamma Ray Spectrometer (GRS) — elemental composition\n6. Magnetometer/Electron Reflectometer (MAG/ER) — magnetic field\n7. Radio Science Experiment (RS) — gravity/atmosphere\n\n**Planned Orbit:** Semi-major axis 3,766 km, eccentricity 0.004, inclination 92.87 deg (polar sun-synchronous), period ~118 min.\n\n**Failure Details:**\n- Probable cause: rupture of fuel (MMH) pressurization tank in propulsion system\n- Mechanism: unintended mixing of nitrogen tetroxide and monomethyl hydrazine in titanium tubing during 11-month cruise, enabled by NTO migration through check valves\n- Rupture released helium and liquid MMH, causing catastrophic spin\n- High spin rate triggered contingency mode, interrupted stored commands, prevented transmitter activation\n- Battery discharged from improper solar array orientation\n\n**Legacy:** All 7 instruments successfully reflown on Mars Global Surveyor (1996), Mars Climate Orbiter (1998), 2001 Mars Odyssey, and Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter (2005).\n\n---\n\n## 1.124 — Clementine (Lunar Water Evidence)\n\n**Launch:** January 25, 1994, 16:34:00 UTC from Vandenberg AFB, SLC-4W\n**Launch Vehicle:** Titan II(23)G\n**Lunar Orbit Insertion:** February 21, 1994\n**Lunar Departure:** May 3, 1994\n**Last Contact:** May 10, 1995\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** Total 424 kg | Dry 227 kg\n**Power:** 1,850 watts\n**Manufacturer:** Naval Research Laboratory\n**Operators:** BMDO (Ballistic Missile Defense Organization) / NASA\n**Official Name:** Deep Space Program Science Experiment (DSPSE)\n\n**Lunar Orbital Parameters:**\n- Polar orbit (90 deg inclination)\n- Periselene: 2,162 km | Aposelene: 4,594 km\n- Period: 300 min (5 hours)\n\n**Instruments:**\n1. Charged Particle Telescope (CPT) — proton/electron flux\n2. Ultraviolet/Visible camera — 5 wavelengths, 288x384 pixel CCD\n3. Near-Infrared CCD Camera — 6 wavelengths, 256x256 pixel, cooled to 70 K\n4. Laser Image Detection and Ranging (LIDAR) — 180 mJ Nd:YAG at 1064 nm\n5. High-Resolution Camera (HIRES) — intensified CCD, 288x384 pixels\n\n**Lunar Water Ice Evidence (Bistatic Radar Experiment):**\n- Radio signals directed at lunar south pole; reflections detected by Deep Space Network\n- Analysis suggested volatile ices including water ice in permanently shadowed craters\n- Subsequent Arecibo observations showed similar patterns from non-shadowed areas, suggesting results may be due to surface roughness\n- Later confirmed by LCROSS mission (2009) and other missions\n\n**Results:**\n- 1.6 million digital images of lunar surface (100-200 m resolution)\n- 300 orbits; mapped 60 deg S to 60 deg N latitude\n- Mission duration: 115 days (lunar phase)\n\n**Failure:** May 7, 1994 — attitude control thruster fired for 11 minutes, spinning spacecraft to ~80 rpm. Prevented planned asteroid 1620 Geographos flyby.\n\n**Propulsion:** Monopropellant hydrazine (attitude) + bipropellant N2O4/MMH (maneuvering); delta-v ~1,900 m/s total.\n\n---\n\n## 1.125 — STS-63 (First Shuttle-Mir Rendezvous)\n\n**Launch:** February 3, 1995, 05:22:04 UTC from KSC LC-39B (night launch)\n**Landing:** February 11, 1995, 11:50:19 UTC at KSC SLF Runway 15\n**Duration:** 8 days, 6 hours, 28 minutes, 15 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Discovery (OV-103)\n\n**Crew (6):**\n- Commander: James Wetherbee (3rd spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Eileen Collins (1st spaceflight) — **first female Shuttle pilot**\n- Payload Commander: Bernard Harris Jr. (2nd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2/Flight Engineer: Michael Foale (3rd spaceflight, US/UK)\n- Mission Specialist 3: Janice Voss (2nd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 4: Vladimir Titov, RKA (3rd spaceflight)\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 275 km | Apogee: 342 km\n- Inclination: 51.6 deg | Period: 92.3 min\n- Orbits: 129 | Distance: 4,816,454 km\n\n**Payload Mass:** 8,641 kg\n\n**Mir Rendezvous (February 6, 1995):**\n- Closest approach: 11 m (36 ft) at 19:23:20 UTC\n- Wetherbee manually controlled approach\n- Mir 17 crew: Commander Viktorenko, FE Kondakova, physician Polyakov\n\n**EVA:** February 9, 1995 — 4 hours, 39 min (Foale and Harris)\n- Bernard Harris: **first African American EVA astronaut**\n- Michael Foale: **first UK-born EVA astronaut**\n- Tested spacesuit thermal modifications; practiced SPARTAN handling\n\n**SPARTAN-204:** Free-flying UV imaging spectrograph, deployed ~40 hours, retrieved.\n\n**SPACEHAB-3:** 20 experiments (11 biotech, 3 materials, 4 tech demos). Included \"Charlotte\" robotic device and Coca-Cola taste perception study.\n\n---\n\n## 1.126 — STS-71 (First Shuttle-Mir Docking)\n\n**Launch:** June 27, 1995, 19:32:19 UTC from KSC LC-39A\n**Landing:** July 7, 1995, 14:55:28 UTC at KSC SLF Runway 15\n**Duration:** 9 days, 19 hours, 23 minutes, 9 seconds\n**Orbiter:** Atlantis (OV-104)\n\n**Launching Crew (7):**\n- Commander: Robert L. \"Hoot\" Gibson (5th spaceflight)\n- Pilot: Charles J. Precourt (2nd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 1: Ellen S. Baker (3rd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 2: Gregory J. Harbaugh (3rd spaceflight)\n- Mission Specialist 3: Bonnie J. Dunbar (4th spaceflight)\n- MS 4: Anatoly Solovyev, RKA (4th spaceflight) — delivered to Mir (EO-19)\n- MS 5: Nikolai Budarin, RKA (1st spaceflight) — delivered to Mir (EO-19)\n\n**Landing Crew (8):** Added Norman Thagard (returning from Mir after 100+ days), Gennady Strekalov, Vladimir Dezhurov (replacing Solovyev and Budarin).\n\n**Orbital Parameters:**\n- Perigee: 342 km | Apogee: 342 km (circular)\n- Inclination: 51.6 deg | Period: 88.9 min\n- Orbits: 153 | Distance: 6,600,000 km\n\n**Payload Mass:** 12,191 kg\n\n**Mir Docking:**\n- Docking: June 29, 1995, 13:00:16 UTC (Kristall module)\n- Undocking: July 4, 1995, 11:09:42 UTC\n- Docked duration: 4 days, 22 hours, 9 minutes, 26 seconds\n- Approach: R-Bar (from directly below), manual control by Gibson\n- Alignment: <25 mm lateral, <0.5 deg angular misalignment per axis\n- Final closure rate: 0.107 ft/s (33 mm/s)\n- **Combined mass: ~225 metric tons — largest spacecraft ever in orbit at that time**\n\n**Spacelab Module:** ~100 hours of joint biomedical operations. 15 investigations across 7 disciplines (cardiovascular, metabolism, neuroscience, radiation, biology, microgravity).\n\n**Supplies Delivered to Mir:** 450+ kg water, spacewalking tools for solar array repairs, oxygen/nitrogen for pressure.\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First Shuttle-space station docking\n- First on-orbit crew exchange (Shuttle/station)\n- 100th U.S. human spaceflight\n- Lowest perigee ever flown by an orbiter (85.3 nmi during OMS-2 burn)\n\n---\n\n## 1.127 — SOHO (Solar and Heliospheric Observatory)\n\n**Launch:** December 2, 1995, 08:08:01 UTC from Cape Canaveral SFS LC-36B\n**Launch Vehicle:** Atlas IIAS (AC-121)\n**L1 Orbit Insertion:** February 14, 1996\n**Current Status:** Still operational (extended through September 2026)\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:** 1,864 kg total | 610 kg payload\n**Dimensions:** 4.3 x 2.7 x 3.7 m (9.5 m with solar arrays)\n**Power:** 1,500 watts\n**Manufacturer:** Matra Marconi Space (now Airbus Defence and Space)\n**Operators:** ESA (mission lead) / NASA (launch, DSN tracking)\n\n**Orbit:** Halo orbit around Sun-Earth L1 point (~1.5 million km from Earth, 0.99 AU from Sun). Orbits L1 every 6 months.\n\n**12 Instruments:**\n\n*Solar Atmosphere Remote Sensing:*\n1. CDS — Coronal Diagnostic Spectrometer (density, temp, flows)\n2. EIT — Extreme ultraviolet Imaging Telescope (coronal structure)\n3. LASCO — Large Angle Spectrometric Coronagraph\n4. SUMER — Solar UV Measurements of Emitted Radiation\n5. SWAN — Solar Wind Anisotropies (hydrogen wavelengths)\n6. UVCS — Ultraviolet Coronagraph Spectrometer\n\n*In-Situ Solar Wind:*\n7. CELIAS — Charge, Element, Isotope Analysis System\n8. COSTEP — Comprehensive Suprathermal and Energetic Particle Analyzer\n\n*Helioseismology:*\n9. GOLF — Global Oscillations at Low Frequencies\n10. MDI — Michelson Doppler Imager (superseded 2011)\n11. VIRGO — Variability of Solar Irradiance and Gravity Oscillations\n\n12. ERNE — Energetic and Relativistic Nuclei and Electron Experiment\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- 5,000+ comets discovered (via LASCO; average 1 every 2.59 days)\n- Near-real-time solar data for space weather prediction\n- Coronal mass ejection tracking and Earth-arrival prediction\n- Internal solar structure and dynamics via helioseismology\n\n**1998 Loss of Contact Incident:**\n- June 24-25, 1998: lost sun lock during gyroscope calibration\n- All contact lost by 04:43 UTC June 25\n- Located via Arecibo/Goldstone radar July 23\n- Carrier signal detected August 3\n- Full recovery completed October 24, 1998\n- Innovation: ESA developed \"gyroless operations mode\" (February 1, 1999) using reaction wheels as virtual gyroscopes — first 3-axis-stabilized spacecraft to operate this way\n\n**Data Transmission:** 200 kbit/s continuous via NASA Deep Space Network\n**Mission Duration:** 30+ years (planned: 3 years). Extended repeatedly.\n\n---\n\n## Summary Statistics\n\n| ID | Mission | Launch Date | Key Achievement |\n|----|---------|-------------|-----------------|\n| 1.101 | Solar Maximum Mission | 1980-02-14
